The Sunnah of Washing Hands Upon Waking Before Wudu
Explains the sunnah of washing hands for one waking from sleep, with hadith proof and intra-Hanafi differences on timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that washing hands upon waking is sunnah mu'akkadah, explain its hadith proof, and identify the Hanafi position on its timing relative to istinja.

Core Idea
Washing hands upon waking is a confirmed sunnah, proven by a hadith whose command is understood as recommendation, not obligation.

This lesson examines the ruling for washing hands before wudu after sleep. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that it is a sunnah mu'akkadah, supported by the well-known hadith. It also clarifies an intra-Hanafi difference regarding whether this washing is recommended before or after istinja. Understanding the distinction between sunnah mu'akkadah and other levels of obligation is key to grasping the Hanafi legal framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is washing the hands up to the wrists three times before inserting them into the wudu vessel. The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, as defined in the text: doer rewarded, abandoner blamed, habitual abandonment sinful.
The definition of sunnah mu'akkadah is critical for understanding the legal weight.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the mutawatir-in-meaning hadith: 'When one of you wakes up from sleep, let him wash his hand before inserting it into his wudu vessel, for none of you knows where his hand spent the night.'
The hadith is narrated by multiple compilers including Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'فليغسل' indicates a command. In Hanafi usul, al-amr yufidu al-wujub. However, the hadith contains a ta'lil (reason) – uncertainty about the hand's location – which serves as a qarina shifting the command from obligation to recommendation, making it sunnah mu'akkadah.
This is an example of how a qarina can override the default rule of amr.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi difference on timing: some scholars (mashayikh) hold it is sunnah before istinja, others after, and the majority say both before and after.
The majority view is considered the most correct (asahh al-aqwal).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator cites al-Muhit al-Burhani, stating that the majority (al-akthar) say it is sunnah absolutely (both before and after istinja). This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
The basis is a narration from the three Sunan authors describing the Prophet's washing practice.

قوله : ثلاثاً؛ هكذا ذكر أكثرهم، والظاهر أنه لو نقص غسلهما عن الثلاث كان آتياً للسنة تاركاً لكمالها، فقد ورد في رواية عند أصحاب السنن في حديث المستيقظ: «فليغسل مرتين أو ثلاثاً».

Book of Purification: Wudu. And its Sunnah: For the one who wakes up, washing his hands up to the wrists three times before inserting them into the vessel. This washing: According to some mashayikh: it is sunnah before istinja. According to others: after it. And according to others: both before and after.
His saying 'its Sunnah': This is in some copies, and in others 'its Sunan' in plural, and what is meant by Sunnah is the confirmed Sunnah (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah), whose ruling is that its doer is rewarded and its abandoner is blamed, and he deserves sin if he habitually leaves it.
His saying 'for the one who wakes up': The restriction is accidental; otherwise, starting with washing the hands is absolutely sunnah.
The basis for this chapter is the hadith: 'When one of you wakes up from sleep, let him wash his hand before inserting it into his wudu vessel, for none of you knows where his hand spent the night.' Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Tirmidhi, Ahmad, Malik, Abu Awana, Tahawi, Darqutni and others with close wordings.
His saying 'three times'; this is what most mentioned, and apparently if he decreases washing them from three, he is performing the sunnah but leaving its perfection, for a narration of the authors of Sunan came in the hadith of the one who wakes up: 'let him wash twice or three times.'
His saying 'both' (jami'an); this is the saying of the majority as in al-Muhit, and it is the most correct of the sayings. The basis for it is what the three authors of Sunan narrated about the prophetic washing: 'that he washed his hands before inserting them into the vessel, then washed...'
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, expanding on the sunnah acts of wudu. The author begins by stating the ruling: for the one who wakes up from sleep, it is sunnah to wash his hands up to the wrists three times before putting them into the vessel of water. The term 'Sunnah' here refers to the confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah), defined as an act for which the doer is rewarded and the abandoner is blamed, and if one habitually abandons it, he becomes sinful. This definition is crucial for understanding the legal weight.

The restriction to 'the one who wakes up' in the matn is considered accidental; the starting act of washing hands in wudu is always sunnah, regardless of whether one has just woken or not. The primary proof is the well-known hadith narrated in multiple collections, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) commands washing the hand before inserting it into the vessel because one does not know where his hand spent the night. This hadith provides the basis for the ruling.

Regarding the number of washings, most scholars say three times. However, a narration mentions 'twice or three times,' so if one washes only twice, he still fulfills the sunnah but has not achieved its perfection. 

There is an intra-Hanafi difference concerning the timing of this washing relative to istinja (cleansing after relieving oneself). Some say it is sunnah before istinja, some say after, and the majority say it is sunnah both before and after. The author states that the majority view, as found in al-Muhit, is that it is sunnah absolutely and is the most correct position. This is supported by narrations of the three Sunan authors describing the Prophet's washing: he washed his hands before inserting them into the vessel, and then washed again after istinja? The text is cut off, but the point is that washing hands is recommended at both points.

Key Terms
السنّة المؤكدة (al-sunnah al-mu'akkadah): A confirmed sunnah: an act that the doer is rewarded for, the abandoner is blamed, and if habitually abandoned, the person becomes sinful. It is between wajib and mustahabb.
الرُسْغ (rusgh): The wrist joint between the forearm and the hand. The washing is required up to this point for the sunnah.
الاستنجاء (al-istinja'): Cleansing the private parts after relieving oneself, using water or stones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on washing hands before wudu for someone who has just woken up?
A) It is wajib, based on the command in the hadith.
B) It is sunnah muakkadah, proven by a hadith but understood as recommendation.
C) It is fard, and omitting it invalidates wudu.
D) It is mubah, with no reward or blame.

Key Takeaway
Washing hands upon waking is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) based on a prophetic hadith, and the Hanafi preferred view recommends it both before and after istinja.

Washing Hands Three Times Upon Waking: Ruling and Evidence
Explains the sunnah of washing hands after sleep and its timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing hands upon waking and explain the evidence and the intra-madhhab difference regarding timing.

Core Idea
Washing hands three times upon waking is sunnah, not wajib; performing less still fulfills the sunnah but abandons its perfection.

In this passage, the commentator explains the sunnah of washing hands after sleep. The matn states 'three times before inserting them into the vessel,' but the commentator expands on the timing relative to istinja', the number of washes, and the evidence from the Prophet's practice. Understanding this ruling helps you appreciate how the Hanafi school derives recommended actions from prophetic habit and the scope of legitimate difference within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its hukm level
Read the passage carefully. What is the act being ruled upon? The washing of hands upon waking. Determine its legal category: is it wajib, sunnah, or something else? The text calls it 'sunnah' and clarifies that doing less is still valid.
Look for key terms like 'sunnah' and the mention of 'perfection' to gauge the level of emphasis.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The commentator cites the hadith: 'Let him wash two or three times.' Also mentions the prophetic practice of washing hands before and after istinja'. Identify the type of evidence: hadith ahad and actual practice (fi'li).
The hadith is from the Sunan collections, so its transmission is zanni, but it is sufficient for a sunnah ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the evidence proves the ruling)
How does the hadith indicate sunnah? The emphatic prohibition and the reason (not knowing where the hand spent the night) point to a recommendation. The variant 'two or three' shows that three is perfect, but two is acceptable.
The Prophet’s consistent practice strengthens the recommendation.
4
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on timing
There are three views: washing before istinja', after istinja', or both. Identify each view and their bases. The majority (and the commentator) prefer both.
This is a legitimate difference within the school; all views have basis in the Prophetic practice.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The commentator states that the majority view is the most correct. So for fatwa, the recommended practice is to wash hands both before and after istinja', three times each (or at least once).
The tarjih is based on the weight of evidence and the practice of most scholars.

Commentary
This passage addresses the sunnah of washing hands upon waking from sleep, specifically for one who intends to perform wudu. The matn states 'three times before inserting them into the vessel.' The commentator discusses the timing: some scholars hold it is sunnah before istinja' (cleansing after relieving oneself), others after istinja', and the majority consider it sunnah both before and after. The majority view is the most correct. Regarding the number, washing three times is the perfect form, but doing less (two or one) still fulfills the sunnah, as evidenced by a variant hadith. The evidence for the entire practice is the prophetic action: he would wash his hands before inserting them into the vessel, then wash his private parts. This is the relied-upon position within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
سنة (sunnah): A recommended practice that the Prophet performed consistently; omitting it is not sinful but losing reward.
استنجاء (istinja'): Cleansing the private parts with water or stones after relieving oneself.
غسل (ghusl): Washing; here specifically washing the hands as part of the sunnah before wudu.

Manner of Washing Hands Upon Waking and the Critique of Mulla Khusraw
Describes prophetic method and addresses objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi recommended method for washing hands upon waking and explain the rational basis for its sequence.

Core Idea
Every detail of wudu is rooted in prophetic practice and legal principles; even transferring moisture is impermissible.

This passage from Umda al-Ri'aya explains the specific method of washing hands after sleep, attributed to the Hanafi jurist Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani. It then addresses an apparent objection: why not simply wash the left hand with the water already on the right? Mulla Khusraw's response reveals two key principles: the legal custom (al-bida'a bi al-yamin) and the prohibition of transferring moisture (naql al-bullah). By examining this critique, we see how Hanafi fiqh preserves prophetic practice through rational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
Determine what is being described (washing hands upon waking) and its legal status. The passage describes a recommended (sunnah muakkadah) method, not an obligatory one.
From the hanafi_position_box, the hukm level is sunnah muakkadah.
2
Trace the method step by step
Follow the sequence: lift small vessel with left, pour on right palm (3x), then pour with right over private parts, wipe earth with both hands, wash both hands, then perform full wudu.
Each step has a purpose; note the left/right alternation.
3
Identify the objection and its rationale
The objection: why repeat pouring for the left hand when it could be washed from the water on the right? The apparent rationale is efficiency.
This objection is from a hypothetical reader or from another school.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Mulla Khusraw gives two reasons: (1) the legal custom is to start with the right, so the left must receive its own fresh water; (2) transferring moisture (naql al-bullah) from one hand to the other is invalid in wudu.
These reasons uphold both prophetic practice (beginning with right) and legal principle (no moisture transfer).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيفية الغسل : أنه إذا كان الإناء صغيراً بحيث يمكن رفعه يرفعه بشماله ، ويصبه على كفه اليمنى، ويغسلها ثلاثاً، ثم يصب بيمينه فرجه، ثم مسح بهما التراب حتى أنقاهما، ثمّ غسل يديه، ثم توضأ وضوءه للصلاة. [قوله : ثم يصب ... الخ، يَرِدُ عليه : أنه لا حاجة إلى تكرار الصب للكفين لإمكان غسلهما بالماء الذي صب على اليمني. وأجاب عنه محمد بن فراموز الشهير بملا خسرو في «الدرر شرح الغرر»: «بأنّ فيه ترجيحاً لعادة العوام على عرف الشرع، فإنّ عرف الشرع البداية باليمين، وبأن نقل البلة في الوضوء من إحدى اليدين أو الرجلين إلى الأخرى لا يجوز))
And the manner of washing: That if the vessel is small such that it can be lifted, he lifts it with his left hand, pours water onto his right palm, and washes it three times, then he pours with his right hand over his private parts, then he wipes the earth with them until he cleans them, then he washes his hands, then he performs wudu as for prayer. [His statement: 'then he pours ... etc.' There is an objection against it: that there is no need to repeat the pouring for the two palms because it is possible to wash them with the water that was poured on the right. And Muhammad ibn Faramuz, known as Mulla Khusraw, responded in 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurrar': 'Because there is a preference for the custom of the common people over the legal custom, for the legal custom is to begin with the right, and because transferring wetness in wudu from one of the hands or feet to the other is not permissible.'
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage explains the detailed method of washing after sleep, attributed to the Hanafi jurist Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani. The method is: if the water vessel is small enough to be lifted, one lifts it with the left hand, pours water over the right hand, washes the right hand three times, then pours water from the right hand over the private parts, then wipes the earth (with both hands) until clean, then washes both hands, then performs wudu. An objection arises: why repeat the pouring of water for the left hand when it could be washed from the water already poured on the right? Mulla Khusraw answers that this method upholds the legal custom of starting with the right hand. Furthermore, transferring moisture from one hand to another in wudu is not permissible, so the left hand must be washed directly with fresh water, not by moisture transferred from the right. This shows the depth of Hanafi fiqh in adhering to prophetic practice and legal principles.

Key Terms
عادة العوام (adat al-'awamm): The common practice of ordinary people, often not aligned with legal customs.
نقل البلة (naql al-bullah): Transferring moisture (water or wetness) from one limb to another during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) To follow the custom of common people.
C) To uphold the legal custom of starting with the right and avoid transferring moisture.
D) Because the left hand cannot be washed by water from the right.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for washing hands upon waking preserves prophetic practice by starting with the right and forbidding moisture transfer; an objection based on convenience is rejected by rational and legal principles.

Manner of Washing Hands at the Beginning of Ghusl
Description of ghusl handwashing procedure with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi procedure for washing hands at the start of ghusl and explain the avoidance of musta'mal water.

Core Idea
Avoid making water musta'mal by inserting only the fingers when the vessel is large.

This lesson focuses on the specific procedure of washing hands at the outset of ghusl (ritual bath) according to the Hanafi school. The ruling distinguishes between small and large water vessels, and addresses the practical concern of preserving the purifying quality of water. We will also examine an intra-Hanafi refinement regarding the principle of tayammun (beginning with the right side).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish vessel sizes
Identify whether the water vessel is small (liftable) or large (not liftable). This determines the hand-washing method.
The ruling adapts to practical constraints while preserving the sunnah.
2
Apply the small vessel procedure
When the vessel is small, lift it with the left hand, pour onto the right palm, wash right hand three times, then transfer the vessel to the right hand and pour onto the left palm, washing left hand three times.
The left lifts to keep the right hand clean for pouring later.
3
Apply the large vessel procedure without a scoop
If the vessel is large and no additional small container is available, insert only the closed fingers of the left hand (not the palm) into the water, pour onto the right hand, rub fingers together, and repeat three times before fully inserting the right hand.
Inserting only fingers avoids rendering the water musta'mal.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi debate on tayammun
Some Hanafi scholars question whether tayammun (preferring the right) applies to the initial hand wash. Ibn Amir Haj argues that the hadiths should be combined and that beginning with the right is not recommended here, similar to washing the cheeks.
The relied-upon position follows the procedure as described, without insisting on tayammun at this step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكيفية الغسل : أنه إذا كان الإناء صغيراً بحيث يمكن رفعه يرفعه بشماله ، ويصبه على كفه اليمنى، ويغسلها ثلاثاً، ثم يصب بيمينه على كفه اليسرى كما ذكرنا. وإن كان كبيراً بحيث لا يمكن رفعه، فإن كان معه إناء صغير، يرفع الماءَ ويغسلهما ثلاثاً كما ذكرنا. وإن لم يكن ، يُدْخِلُ أصابع يده اليسرى مضمومة في الإناء، ولا يدخلُ الكف، ويصب الماء على يمينه ، ويدلك الأصابع بعضها ببعض يفعل هكذا ثلاثاً ، ثم يدخلُ يمناه في الإناء بالغاً ما بلغ. وفيه ما فيه، والحق ما قال محمد بن محمد بن محمد الشهير بابن أمير حاج، تلميذ ابن الهمام في «شرح منية المصلي» المسمّى بـ «حَلْبة المجلّي»: «إنّ ظاهر الأحاديث الجمع بينها، وقد نص غير علمائنا على أنه لا يستحب التيامن هاهنا كما في غسل الخدين ، عنه». انتهى (1) ومح مسح الأذنين والخفين والقواعد لا تنبو .
And the manner of ghusl: that if the vessel is small such that it can be lifted, he lifts it with his left hand and pours it onto his right palm and washes it three times, then he pours with his right hand onto his left palm as we mentioned. And if it is large such that it cannot be lifted, then if he has a small vessel with him, he lifts the water and washes them both three times as we mentioned. And if he does not have (a small vessel), he inserts the fingers of his left hand, closed together, into the vessel—and he does not insert the palm—and pours water onto his right hand, and rubs the fingers together. He does this three times, then he inserts his right hand into the vessel, as far as it goes. And in it there is what there is, and the truth is what Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad, known as Ibn Amir Haj, the student of Ibn al-Humam, said in 'Sharh Munyat al-Musalli' named 'Halbat al-Mujalli': 'Indeed the apparent meaning of the hadiths is to combine them, and some of our scholars have stated that it is not recommended to begin with the right here as in washing the cheeks...' end. And wiping the ears and the socks and the principles do not refuse.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the manner of washing the hands at the beginning of ghusl (ritual bath). It is part of a commentary (sharh) on the text of Al-Wiqayah. The author explains the detailed procedure according to the Hanafi school, transmitted from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani. The passage distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) When the water vessel is small and can be lifted: one lifts it with the left hand, pours water onto the right palm, washes the right hand three times, then transfers the vessel to the right hand and pours onto the left palm, washing it three times. (2) When the vessel is large and cannot be lifted: if one has a separate small vessel, one uses it to scoop water and washes hands as before. If no small vessel is available, one inserts only the fingers of the left hand (not the palm) into the vessel, keeping them together to avoid making the water used (musta'mal). Then one pours water from the left fingers onto the right hand and rubs the fingers together. This is done three times before inserting the right hand fully into the vessel. The commentary then notes a refinement by Ibn Amir Haj (student of Ibn al-Humam) who argues that the ahadith should be reconciled, and that some Hanafi scholars hold that beginning with the right (tayammun) is not recommended here, similar to washing the cheeks (which are done together). This shows the intra-Hanafi nuance in deriving the ruling from the primary texts. The passage demonstrates the importance of avoiding making water musta'mal (used) in ghusl by not inserting the entire hand unnecessarily.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): The ritual bath required to remove major ritual impurity (janabah, hayd, nifas).
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has already been used for a valid act of purification and thus loses its purifying quality.
تيامن (tayammun): The act of beginning with the right side or right limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Insert the entire left hand into the vessel.
B) Insert only the fingers of the left hand, keeping them together, into the vessel.
C) Pour water from the vessel directly onto the right hand without touching the vessel.
D) Use the right hand to scoop water and wash both hands.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method for washing hands at the start of ghusl is a sunnah mu'akkadah, with a careful procedure to avoid making the water musta'mal, and the intra-Hanafi debate on tayammun is resolved by combining hadith evidence.

Conditional Prohibition: Dipping Hand Before Washing
Commentary on the ruling of the hadith prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith prohibiting dipping the hand before washing and explain when the prohibition applies conditionally.

Core Idea
The hadith's prohibition is not absolute — it applies only when no genuine need exists.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains how to understand the Prophet's prohibition of dipping the hand into the purifier before washing. The commentator clarifies that the ruling depends on necessity, a key feature of Hanafi methodology that balances textual evidence with practical need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual proof
Note the hadith cited by al-Bazzar: 'When one of you wakes from his sleep, he should not dip his hand into his purifier until he pours water over it.' This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
2
Recognize the implicit objection
The commentator addresses an implied objection: the absolute wording of the hadith suggests it applies in all cases. Why then do Hanafis permit dipping in some cases?
This objection is a common point of critique from other madhahib.
3
Follow the commentator's resolution
The prohibition is interpreted as applying only when there is no need (hajah). When need exists (e.g., large vessel and no other container), the prohibition is understood as forbidding only excessive dipping, not the act itself.
Need here is a customarily recognized necessity.
4
Understand the linguistic move
To avoid combining literal and figurative meanings (jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz), the commentator takes the broader meaning: 'do not dip more than necessary,' which applies in all scenarios.
This reflects Hanafi usul preference for avoiding unnecessary figurative interpretation.
5
Note the different hadith wordings
The commentator cites variant narrations: with emphatic noon, without noon, and 'la yadkhul' (do not insert), showing the hadith's widespread transmission.
Variations do not affect the core ruling.

And the prohibition in his saying: 'He should not dip his hand into the vessel' is interpreted as applying when the vessel is small or large and with it there is a small vessel. [1] His saying: 'and the prohibition... etc.' is a rebuttal of an implied objection: that his (peace be upon him) prohibition of dipping the hand into the vessel before washing it—i.e., inserting it—by its absolute nature necessitates that insertion of the hand before washing is not permissible in any case, so how have you permitted it in some cases? And the estimated answer: that it is interpreted as applying when there is no need for it, i.e., when the vessel is small or large with a small vessel along with it; but when there is need, as in the last scenario, it is interpreted as a prohibition of inserting more than necessary, not absolutely. If you say: then it would entail combining the literal and figurative meaning. I say: it is interpreted in the broader sense, and the meaning is: he should not dip his hand into the vessel more than necessary, and this is found in every estimation. [2] His saying: 'fa-la yaghmissanna' is with the emphatic noon, according to what al-Bazzar recorded in his Musnad: 'When one of you wakes from his sleep, he should not dip his hand into his purifier until he pours water over it.' In another narration: 'fa-la yaghmis' without the noon, and in most narrations: 'fa-la yadkhul.' [3] His saying: 'interpreted as applying'; and as for what Sa'id ibn Mansur recorded from Ibn 'Umar... he saw him
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn, likely from Al-Wiqaya, discussing the hadith: 'He should not dip his hand into the vessel.' The commentator explains that the prohibition is not absolute but conditional. It applies only when there is no need for dipping the hand, such as when the vessel is small or when there is another small vessel available. However, when there is a genuine need—like a large vessel with no other container—the prohibition is understood as forbidding only excessive dipping, not the act itself. To address a potential objection that this interpretation combines literal and figurative meaning, the commentator argues that it takes the broader meaning: 'do not dip more than necessary.' This is consistent across all cases. The commentator also cites the hadith sources: al-Bazzar reports it with the emphatic noon, while others report it without, or as 'do not insert.' This shows the commentator's concern for both textual proof and rational interpretation, a hallmark of Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition from the Prophet (SAW), here interpreted as conditional, not absolute.
الحاجة (al-hājah): Need that permits an otherwise prohibited action, such as when the vessel is large and no other container is available.
الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز (al-jam‘ bayn al-ḥaqīqah wa al-majāz): Combining literal and figurative interpretation of the same text, which the commentator avoids by taking the broader meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith's prohibition conditionally, allowing dipping when need arises, by taking the broader meaning of the text.

Interpreting the Hadith on Hand-Dipping After Sleep: Prohibition or Dislike?
Expansion on the interpretation of the hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dipping the hand into a vessel after waking, explain the contextual interpretation of the hadith, and distinguish between a prohibitory (tahrimi) and non-prohibitory (tanzihi) prohibition.

Core Idea
The hadith's prohibition is interpreted contextually as makruh tanzih, not haram, unless there is genuine need.

The hadith 'Do not dip your hand into the vessel until you wash it' appears to be an absolute prohibition. However, the Hanafi school, through careful application of usul al-fiqh and consideration of the Companions' actions, derives a nuanced ruling. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains how the prohibition is interpreted contextually, making it disliked (makruh tanzih) rather than unlawful (haram).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and apparent ruling
The act is dipping the hand into a water vessel after waking from sleep before washing the hand. The apparent ruling from the hadith is prohibition (nahy).
The hadith uses emphatic language: 'fala yaghmisanna'.
2
Locate the hadith and note textual variations
The hadith is narrated by al-Bazzār and others. Variations include 'fala yaghmis' without emphasis and 'fala yudkhil' (do not insert). These variations help in interpretation.
The presence of multiple wordings suggests the prohibition is not rigidly fixed.
3
Analyse the ta'lil (reason) in the hadith
The Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'For indeed one of you does not know where his hand spent the night.' This reason indicates ritual impurity (hadath) rather than physical impurity (najāsah), which is a lesser degree.
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition with a mild reason may be interpreted as tanzihi (disliked) rather than tahrimi (prohibitory).
4
Consider the actions of the Companions and need-based exceptions
The passage cites that Ibn 'Umar and al-Barā' dipped their hands before washing, showing the prohibition is not absolute. Also, if the vessel is large and no alternative exists, the prohibition is interpreted as forbidding excess beyond need.
Need (hājah) makes it permissible to dip the hand to the extent required.
5
Derive the Hanafi ruling with conditions
The final ruling: makruh tanzih to dip the hand without washing when no need; permissible with need. If known najāsah is on the hand, it becomes obligatory to remove it without contaminating the vessel.
This ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

And the estimation of the answer: That it is interpreted as when there is no need, which is when the vessel is small or large and he has a small one. But if there is need, as in the latter case, it is interpreted as a prohibition of inserting more than the amount of need, not absolutely. So the prohibition... I say: It is interpreted in the more general sense, and the meaning is: He should not dip his hand into the vessel beyond the amount of need, and this exists in every estimation. All of this applies if he does not know of any impurity on his hand. But if he knows, then removing the impurity in a way that does not lead to contaminating the vessel or other things is obligatory. [2] (And the saying of Bismillah at the beginning)... He inserted his hand into the vessel before washing it, and Ibn Abī Shaybah narrated from al-Barā': 'He inserted his hand into the water vessel before washing it.' This is interpreted as indicating permissibility, and as an indication that the prohibition in the hadith is non-prohibitory (tanzīhī), as its justification (ta'līl) by his saying: 'For indeed one of you does not know where his hand spent the night' requires. [1] His saying: 'Removing the impurity': So he commands someone else to scoop and pour. If he does not find anyone, he inserts a cloth into it and removes the impurity by what drips from it. If he does not find, he lifts water with his mouth. If he is unable to do anything, he performs tayammum and prays. This is as in Jāmi' al-Mudmarāt.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of dipping one's hand into a water vessel after waking up from sleep, before washing the hand. The sharh explains that the apparent prohibition in the hadith is not absolute but is interpreted contextually. If the vessel is small, or if it is large but one has another small vessel, the prohibition applies to avoid wasting water. If one has no alternative and needs to use the same water, the prohibition is understood as forbidding excess dipping beyond necessity. The commentator addresses a potential objection about combining literal and figurative interpretation by adopting a general meaning that includes both. The hadith from al-Bazzār is cited with the emphatic 'noon', and other narrations show variations. The passage also mentions that acts of the Companions like Ibn 'Umar and al-Barā' dipping their hands before washing indicate permissibility, showing the prohibition is of a non-prohibitory nature (tanzīhī), as the reason given is one of ritual impurity (not knowing where the hand spent the night) rather than intrinsic impurity. If known impurity (najāsah) is on the hand, then it becomes obligatory to remove it without contaminating the vessel, and the text provides practical methods: asking another to pour, using a cloth, using mouth to lift water, or finally tayammum.

Key Terms
غمس (ghams): Dipping or immersing the hand into water.
محمول (maml): Interpreted or restricted in meaning based on context or evidence.
تنزيهي (tanzihi): A prohibition that is non-prohibitory, indicating dislike (karahah) rather than unlawfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith's prohibition as makruh tanzih, not haram, due to the mild reason and Companion practice, allowing flexibility based on need.

The Ruling of Tasmiyah in Wudu: Three Hanafi Opinions
Three Hanafi opinions on saying Bismillah in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rulings on saying Bismillah during wudu and identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.

Core Idea
Saying Bismillah at the start of wudu is sunnah muakkadah, not wajib — the Hanafi majority position, despite weaker opinions.

When commencing wudu, many Muslims recite 'Bismillah' out of habit. But what is its legal status? This passage from the sharh reveals an intra-madhhab dispute among Hanafi scholars, with three distinct views. Understanding this nuance helps distinguish between obligatory and recommended acts in purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The act is 'tasmiyah' – saying 'Bismillah' – at the beginning of wudu. Note that the passage explicitly states that the scholars differed on this point.
2
List the three opinions
The passage mentions two opinions explicitly: (1) mustahabb (recommended) – weak; (2) sunnah muakkadah (confirmed sunnah) – majority. A third opinion, implied, is that it is wajib (obligatory).
The third opinion is mentioned in the context of cross-madhhab disputes but is also present within the Hanafi school as a minority view.
3
Determine the majority and mufta bihi
According to the passage, the majority of Hanafi scholars hold it as sunnah muakkadah. This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position and the mufta bihi.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah authenticated the mustahabb opinion, but it is considered weak; the majority prevails.
4
Note the practical consequence
If one intentionally omits tasmiyah, according to the Hanafi school, the wudu is valid but disliked (makruh). This is in contrast to the Hanbali view that it would be invalid.
The difference shows the importance of knowing the madhhab's ruling.

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the ruling on reciting Bismillah at the start of wudu. It presents three opinions within the Hanafi school. The third opinion is not explicitly mentioned in this excerpt, but the text alludes to a third: the text says 'three statements' and lists two; the third is likely that it is wajib (obligatory), which is a view held by some but not the relied-upon position. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it is sunnah muakkadah.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' at the beginning of an action, here specifically at the start of wudu.
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah muakkadah): A confirmed sunnah, meaning an action the Prophet regularly performed and emphasized, omission of which is disliked but not punishable.
مستحب (mustahabb): Recommended but not emphasized; its omission is not disliked.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi on tasmiyah in wudu is sunnah muakkadah, meaning it is a confirmed sunnah but not obligatory.

Removing Impurity Without Contaminating a Large Vessel
Explains ruling and methods for removing impurity from hand in a large vessel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling and describe the stepwise methods for removing impurity from the hand when the vessel is large, according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
When the hand is impure and the vessel is large, removing impurity is fard; you must avoid contaminating the water by using alternative methods.

In Hanafi fiqh, water purity is preserved carefully. If a person has impurity on their hand and the only water available is in a large vessel, they cannot simply wash it directly. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling and the sequential methods to fulfill the obligation of purification without spoiling the water for others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage applies when: (1) the person knows (not doubts) there is impurity on the hand, (2) the water vessel is large, and (3) there is no small vessel to pour water separately.
2
State the ruling
The text explicitly says: 'فإزالة النجاسة... فرض' — removing impurity is fard (obligatory). The method must not lead to contaminating the vessel or other water.
3
List the methods in order of priority
1) Order someone else to scoop water and pour it over the impurity. 2) If no one is available, insert a clean cloth into the water and let the drips wash the impurity. 3) If no cloth, draw water with the mouth. 4) If completely unable, perform tayammum and pray.
The order shows a progression from using another person to using one's own body, always preserving the water's purity.
4
Understand the fallback
If none of the methods are possible, tayammum becomes permissible as a substitute for wudu or ghusl needed for prayer. This is based on the principle that inability to purify with water allows dry purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسمية الله تعالى ابتداءً أما إذا كان الإناء كبيراً، وليس معه إناء صغير، يحمل على الإدخال بطريق المبالغة ، وكل ذلك إذا لم يعلم على يدِهِ نجاسة، أما إذا عَلِمَ فإزالةُ النَّجاسة على وجه لا يفضي إلى تنجيس الإناء أو غيره فرض. قوله : فإزالة النجاسة ؛ فيأمر غيره بالاغترافِ والصب، فإن لم يجد أدخل فيه ثوباً، ويزيل النجاسة بما تقاطر منه، فإن لم يجد رفع الماء بفيه، فإن لم يقدر على شيء تيمم وصلّى. كذا في «جامع المضمرات».
And saying the name of Allah at the beginning? As for when the vessel is large and there is no small vessel with him, it (the hand) is considered as inserted in an exaggerated manner. And all that is when he does not know of any impurity on his hand. But if he knows, then removing the impurity in a way that does not lead to contaminating the vessel or other than it is fard. He said: 'Removing the impurity': so he orders someone else to scoop and pour. If he does not find (anyone), he inserts a cloth into it (the water) and removes the impurity by what drips from it. If he does not find (a cloth), he lifts the water with his mouth. If he is unable to do anything, he performs tayammum and prays. Thus in 'Jami' al-Madhmurat'.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case in purification: a person has impurity on his hand, the water vessel is large, and there is no small vessel to pour water. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: if he does not know of impurity (i.e., he only doubts), his hand is considered as having entered the water in an exaggerated sense (i.e., the water remains pure), but if he knows for certain there is impurity, removing it is obligatory (fard), and he must do so without contaminating the water. The commentary then explains the method: he should have someone else scoop water for him; if no one is available, he can insert a cloth to let water drip onto the impurity; if no cloth, he can draw water with his mouth; if completely unable, he resorts to tayammum. This shows the Hanafi principle of avoiding contamination of still water while fulfilling the obligation of purity.

Key Terms
الإناء الكبير (al-inā' al-kabīr): A water vessel large enough that hand insertion does not necessarily cause water to flow back into the vessel
إزالة النجاسة (izālat al-najāsah): The obligatory act of removing physical impurity from oneself or garment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on removing impurity from the hand when one knows there is impurity and the vessel is large?
A) It is mustahab
B) It is fard
C) It is makruh
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes preserving the purity of water for others, even at the cost of requiring alternative purification methods.

The Three Hanafi Opinions on Tasmiyah at the Start of Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute with three rulings and dalil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on tasmiyah at wudu, identify the evidence cited, and explain why the author prefers the wajib opinion.

Core Idea
Saying Bismillah before wudu is either mustahab, sunnah mu'akkadah, or wajib – the author favors wajib.

When beginning wudu, should one say 'Bismillah'? Within the Hanafi school, there is a well-known internal dispute on the exact ruling. This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`ayah presents three distinct opinions, ranging from merely recommended to obligatory. The author analyzes the key hadith evidence and gives his preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its disputed ruling
The act is saying 'Bismillah' at the start of wudu (tasmiyah). The ruling is disputed: is it mustahab, sunnah mu'akkadah, or wajib?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
List the three opinions and their proponents
First: mustahab (weak, but Sahib al-Hidayah held it). Second: sunnah mu'akkadah (majority of Hanafis). Third: wajib (Ibn al-Humam and the author).
Note the author's tarjih towards wajib.
3
Examine the primary dalil
The hadith 'La wudu' liman lam yadhhukr ism Allah 'alayh' is cited. In Hanafi usul, negation of validity (la wudu) implies that the missing element (tasmiyah) is a condition or wajib.
The hadith is ahad, so thubut is zanni; dalala is also zanni, but the author argues it is strong.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
If tasmiyah is wajib, omitting it deliberately makes the wudu makruh tahrimi (though still valid). If sunnah mu'akkadah, omission is makruh tanzihi.
The practical difference lies in the degree of dislikedness.
5
Note the author's personal tarjih
The author explicitly says wajib is 'al-awfaq bil-manqul wal-usul' (most consistent with transmitted evidence and principles) and wrote a treatise on it.
Despite this, the established mufta bihi in the school remains sunnah mu'akkadah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وتسمية الله ؛ اختلف أصحابنا فيه على ثلاثة أقوال : الأول : إنه مستحب، وهو قول ضعيف، وإن صححه صاحب «الهداية». والثاني : إنه سنّة مؤكدة، وعليه أكثرهم. والثالث : إنه واجب ، وإليه مال ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير»، وهو الأوفقُ بالمنقول والأصول. والأصل فيه حديث: «لا وضوء لمن لا يذكر اسم الله عليه» ، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي والدارقطني وابن ماجه وغيرهم، وروى البزار : «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا بدأ الوضوءَ سمّى»، ولفظ : «بسم ا الله الرحمن الرحيم» أو «بسم الله العظيم والحمد لله على دين الإسلام ، وبهذا وردت بعض الآثار، وقد فصلت هذه المسألة بدلائلها واختلافتها وتفريعاتها في رسالتي : إحكام القنطرة في أحكام البسملة).
[2] He said: 'And saying the name of Allah'; our companions differed concerning it on three opinions: First: It is mustahab, and it is a weak opinion, even though the author of al-Hidayah declared it sound. Second: It is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah), and this is the view of the majority. Third: It is obligatory (wajib), and Ibn al-Humam inclined to this in Fath al-Qadir, and it is the most consistent with transmitted evidence and principles. The basis for it is the hadith: 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it' reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Daraqutni, Ibn Majah, and others. Al-Bazzar narrated: 'When the Messenger of Allah started wudu, he would say Bismillah.' The wording is 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' or 'Bismillah al-'Azim wal-hamdu lillah 'ala din al-Islam', and some traditions are reported with this. I have detailed this issue with its proofs, differences, and derivations in my treatise Ihkam al-Qantara fi Ahkam al-Basmalah.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the internal Hanafi dispute on the ruling of saying Bismillah at the beginning of wudu. Three views are recorded: (1) Mustahab — weak; (2) Sunnah mu'akkadah — majority view; (3) Wajib — preferred by Ibn al-Humam and deemed most consistent with the sources. The author supports the third view. The primary evidence is the hadith 'La wudu' liman lam yadhhukr ism Allah 'alayh' which uses the negation of wudu, implying wujub. The author mentions his own treatise on the subject. This is an intra-madhhab dispute; the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school is often the second (sunnah mu'akkadah), but the author sides with the third.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' at the beginning of an action, specifically at the start of wudu.
سنّة مؤكدة (sunnah mu'akkadah): A strongly recommended practice that the Prophet consistently performed and rarely omitted.
مستحب (mustahab): Recommended but not emphasized; omission without reason is not disliked.
لا وضوء لمن لا يذكر اسم الله عليه (la wudu' li-man lam yadhhukr ism Allah 'alayh): Hadith: 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it.'

The Linguistic and Legal Definition of Siwak
Linguistic definition and legal implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state both the linguistic and legal definitions of siwak and explain why the sunnah applies to the action of using it, not to possessing the stick.

Core Idea
Siwak is both the stick and the action — the sunnah attaches to the action, not to possessing the stick.

This short passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah resolves a common point of confusion: does the sunnah of siwak mean owning a miswak stick, or using it? By examining the dual linguistic usage of the word 'siwak' itself, the author clarifies that the sunnah is the act of using it. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the ruling in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'siwak'
The word 'siwak' can refer to the physical stick used to clean the teeth, and also to the very act of using that stick. Recognize that it serves both as a noun and a verbal noun.
The author cites al-Misbah as authority for this dual usage.
2
Understand the grammatical point (hadhf al-mudhaf)
Some scholars thought that when the matn says 'wa al-siwaak', it means 'sunnah of siwak' by way of elision (hadhf al-mudhaf). The author argues this is unnecessary because the word 'siwak' already includes the meaning of the act.
The debate is whether a missing word must be assumed; the author says no.
3
Derive the legal implication
Because siwak can mean the act, the sunnah ruled by the shari‘ah is the action of using the siwak, not merely possessing the stick. This affects how a Muslim earns the reward.
The passage concludes with 'السنة استعماله لا نفس السواك'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسواك : هو بالكسر اسم لعود يدلك على الأسنان، ومَصْدَرٌ أيضاً بمعنى الاستياك بالسواك، كما في «المصباح» ، وحينئذ فلا حاجة إلى حذف المضاف، بناء على أن السنة استعماله لا نفس السواك، كما صدر عن جمع من الناظرين).
And siwak (with kasra) is a noun for a stick with which one rubs the teeth, and also a verbal noun meaning the act of using the siwak, as in al-Misbah. Accordingly, there is no need to assume an omitted genitive construction (i.e., 'the sunnah of using siwak'), based on the fact that the sunnah is its use, not the siwak itself, as has been stated by a number of commentators.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of siwak. It is both the actual stick and the act of using it. The author points out that some commentators assumed an omitted word (i.e., 'sunnah of using siwak'), but this is unnecessary because the sunnah is the act of using the siwak, not the physical object itself. This is important for legal precision: the sunnah ruling applies to the action, not to possessing the stick. The source al-Misbah is cited as authority for this linguistic point. The passage is from a commentary on al-Wiqayah, discussing the wording 'والسواك' in the original text.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwaak): A stick used for cleaning teeth; also the act of using it
حذف المضاف (hadhf al-mudhaf): Omission of a word in a genitive construction (idafah) to imply a broader meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Basmalah in Wudu: Fard, Sunnah, or Wajib
Presents three legal positions on basmalah in wudu with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three positions on basmalah in wudu, explain the Hanafi majority view, and identify the evidence used by each school.

Core Idea
The Basmalah in wudu: Hanafis say sunnah, Shafi'is say fard, and some Hanafis say wajib — all from the same hadith.

This passage tackles a classic inter-madhhab dispute: whether saying 'Bismillah' before wudu is obligatory or merely recommended. The Hanafi school is further divided between sunnah and wajib. Let's unpack the evidence and reasoning to see how different scholars arrive at different rulings from the same source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three positions mentioned: (1) fard (Shafi'i/Hanbali), (2) sunnah (majority Hanafi), (3) wajib (Ibn al-Humam, Hanafi). Note who holds each.
The text explicitly labels the third as 'wajib' and says it is more consistent with transmitted evidence.
2
Examine the primary hadith
Find the hadith quoted: 'La wudu'a liman lam yadhkur ism Allah 'alayh' (There is no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name upon it). Recognize that this is the key source.
The hadith is reported by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, etc. Another hadith from al-Bazzar describes the Prophet saying basmalah when beginning wudu.
3
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The majority Hanafi position interprets the imperative as istihbab (recommendation), not wujub (obligation). They point to other evidences where the Prophet did not mention basmalah, implying it is not obligatory.
The text states the author's own view that Ibn al-Humam's wajib position is more consistent, but the mufta bihi remains sunnah.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference
Ibn al-Humam argues for wajib based on the strength of the hadith and istihsan (juristic preference). Compare this with the majority view.
This illustrates that even within a madhhab, ikhtilaf can exist on lesser issues.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
For a Shafi'i, omitting basmalah invalidates wudu. For a Hanafi (majority), wudu is valid but reward is missed. For those following Ibn al-Humam, it is sinful to omit intentionally without excuse.
The practical difference affects validity in worship across madhahib.

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the ruling of saying 'Bismillah' (basmalah) when beginning wudu. The author, a Hanafi commentator, reports three positions: (1) that it is fard (obligatory) – the view held by Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad; (2) that it is sunnah – the majority position of the Hanafi school; and (3) that it is wajib – a strong opinion within the Hanafi school adopted by Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir, which the author says is more consistent with transmitted evidence and principles. The primary evidence cited is the hadith 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it,' which the Hanafis interpret as indicating sunnah when combined with other evidences, while other schools take it as indicating fard. The author also mentions his own treatise on the subject. The commentary aims to clarify the basis of each position.

البسملة (al-basmalah): The act of saying 'Bismillah' (بسم الله) at the beginning of an action, specifically here at the start of wudu.

Siwaak: Definition, Ruling, Times, and Method
Defines siwaak, states its sunnah ruling, preferred times, method, and permissibility of using another's.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define siwaak, state its ruling as sunnah muakkadah with evidence, apply the preferred method (widthwise for teeth, lengthwise for tongue), and explain the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on methodology.

Core Idea
The sunnah of siwaak is the act, not the stick — and widthwise on teeth is best.

Siwaak is a beloved practice of the Prophet ﷺ, combining oral hygiene and spiritual reward. In this lesson, we unpack its definition, legal status, recommended times, and the fine points of how to use it. We also look at a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school on the method, so you can confidently apply the preferred way.

Guided Path
1
Define siwaak linguistically and legally
First, understand that 'siwaak' can mean the stick itself or the action of cleaning teeth. The text clarifies that the sunnah is the action (istiyak), not merely possessing the stick.
This distinction prevents misunderstanding the sunnah’s nature.
2
State the ruling and its evidence
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (confirmed sunnah). The evidence comes from many hadiths, both verbal encouragements and the Prophet's practice, as detailed in books like al-Si'aya.
The source mentions 'the six authentic books' as references.
3
Identify the preferred times
Siwaak is recommended during wudu at the moment of rinsing the mouth (madamada) and also before every prayer. These are the two emphasized occasions.
It is not limited to these times but is especially meritorious here.
4
Analyze the method: widthwise vs lengthwise
The text presents two views: the preferred (al-awla) according to al-Halba is widthwise for teeth and lengthwise for the tongue. Another view says the opposite, and this is said to be the majority. Learn the tarjih and why it is preferred.
This is an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf; both methods are valid, but the first is better.
5
Note the ruling on using another's siwaak
It is permissible to use someone else's siwaak if they give permission. The notion of karahah (dislike) is baseless, as stated in al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya.
This corrects a common misconception.

(۷) «المصباح»(ص ۲۹۷). (v) ويجوز الاستياك بسواكِ غيره إن أذن له ) ، ولا عبرة لما اشتهر من الكراهة ، صرح به في «الفتاوى الخيرية» (۸) ، وحقيقته في رسالتي : «إفادة الخير في الاستياك بسواك الغير».
His saying: 'al-siwaak' – it is, with kasra (siwaak), a noun for a stick with which one rubs the teeth, and also a verbal noun meaning the act of using the siwaak, as in al-Misbah. Then there is no need to delete the addendum (in the phrase), based on the fact that the sunnah is its usage, not the siwaak itself, as a group of scholars have stated. The origin of it is the verbal hadiths encouraging the use of siwaak, and the practical hadiths narrated in the six authentic books and others, as we detailed in al-Si'aya. Its time in wudu is when rinsing the mouth (madamada), and it is also recommended at every prayer. The preferred method is to use it widthwise, not lengthwise, as in al-Mujtaba. And it is said: the opposite, and the majority adopt that, as in al-Bahr. And the best, as in al-Halba, is to use it widthwise for the teeth and lengthwise for the tongue. And it is permissible to use someone else's siwaak if he gives permission, and there is no regard for the famous opinion of dislike; it is stated clearly in al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya. And its reality is in my treatise: Ifadat al-Khair fi al-Istiyak bi Siwaak al-Ghayr.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of siwaak. First, it defines siwaak both as the stick (noun) and the action of using it (verbal noun). The key point is that the sunnah is the act of cleaning with the siwaak, not the stick itself, so there is no need to assume an omission in the text (i.e., saying 'siwaak' implies 'using siwaak'). The evidence for siwaak comes from hadiths, both verbal encouragements and the Prophet's own practice. The recommended time for siwaak is during wudu at the moment of madamada (rinsing the mouth), and also before every prayer. Regarding the method: the preferred way is to use the siwaak widthwise across the teeth, and lengthwise for the tongue. There is a difference of opinion; some say the opposite, but the majority hold to widthwise for teeth. Finally, it is permissible to use another person's siwaak with their permission, contrary to a commonly held notion of dislike.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwaak): A stick or twig used to clean the teeth; also the act of using it.
الاستياك (al-istiyak): The act of using the siwaak to clean the teeth.
المضمضة (al-madamada): Rinsing the mouth with water; the recommended time for siwaak during wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) At the start of wudu

Key Takeaway
Siwaak is a confirmed sunnah; the best method is widthwise on teeth and lengthwise on tongue, and it is recommended at every prayer and during wudu at madamada.

Definition and Proof of Madamada in Wudu
Defines madamada linguistically and technically, with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define madamada linguistically and technically, and identify the proof for its practice in wudu according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Madamada is sunnah in wudu, defined as fully rinsing the mouth, proven by the Prophet's practice.

In this lesson, we explore the definition of madamada (rinsing the mouth) both linguistically and in fiqh terminology. We also see the evidence that establishes its place in wudu, which in the Hanafi school is at the level of sunnah, not obligation.

Guided Path
1
Define madamada linguistically
The text says 'lughatan: tahrik al-ma'' meaning moving water in the mouth. Understand that the root word implies movement.
The linguistic meaning is broader than the technical.
2
Define madamada technically
The text says 'istilahan: isti'ab al-ma' jami' al-fam' meaning making water reach the entire mouth. This is the fiqh definition.
This definition distinguishes it from mere gargling.
3
Identify the proof
The text states that madamada and istinshaq are established by everyone who narrated the Prophet's wudu. This indicates tawatur or at least wide transmission.
The proof is from the Prophet's action, which in Hanafi usul indicates sunnah when not accompanied by a command.
4
Determine the hukm in the Hanafi school
Although not explicitly stated in this passage, the Hanafi position is that madamada is sunnah, not wajib. The proof is from the Prophet's practice, which is not in the form of a command that would indicate obligation.
Other schools may consider it wajib; the difference stems from usul al-fiqh principles.

The passage serves as a definition and proof of their practice.

Renewing Water for Madmadah and Istinshaq: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Commentary on the dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding renewing water for madmadah and istinshaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on renewing water for madmadah and istinshaq and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Renew water for each rinse and sniff — that is the Hanafi sunnah; the Shafi'i connects, but both are valid.

This passage from 'Umdah al-Ri'ayah clarifies the sunnah of madmadah and istinshaq in wudu, focusing on whether to use fresh water for each act or to combine them. The commentary explains that the matn's choice of the word 'bimiyah' (with waters) over 'three times' deliberately indicates the preferred practice of renewing water each time. The Hanafi school considers this the sunnah, while the Shafi'i school considers connecting (al-wasl) as sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on (madmadah and istinshaq) and classify the hukm level (sunnah muakkadah).
The passage focuses on the sunnah method, not the obligatory aspects.
2
Analyze the linguistic indicator
Examine why the matn uses 'bimiyah' (with waters) instead of 'three times'. Understand how the plural form implies diversity and renewal.
This is a key usul point: the wording of the matn is intentionally chosen to convey the ruling.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The author relies on practical hadiths, particularly those narrated in the six authentic books, showing the Prophet used fresh water for each rinse and sniff.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah, but sufficient for a sunnah ruling.
4
Contrast with the opposing school
Identify the Shafi'i (and Hanbali) position that the sunnah is to connect (use one handful for both mouth and nose three times). Know their evidence from the hadith of 'Uthman.
Both schools have valid evidence; the dispute is over which is the optimal sunnah.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Apply the ruling: A Hanafi should take separate water for each of the three rinses and three sniffs. If someone connects, their wudu is valid but they lose the sunnah reward.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the preferred vs. permissible method.

And rinsing the mouth: its basis is the verbal hadiths encouraging siwak, and the practical ones transmitted in the six authentic books and others, as we have elaborated in al-Si'ayah. Its time in wudu is at the time of rinsing, and it is also recommended at every prayer. The recommended (mandub) is to use the siwak horizontally, not vertically, as in al-Mujtaba; and it is said the opposite, and the majority are upon that, as in al-Bahr. The better way, as in al-Hulba, is to use it horizontally on the teeth and vertically on the tongue. It is permissible to use someone else's siwak if he permits, and no attention is paid to the prevalent notion of dislike; this is explicitly stated in al-Fatawa al-Khayriyyah, and its reality is in my treatise: Ifadat al-Khayr fi al-Istiyak bi Siwak al-Ghayr. His saying: 'al-madmadah' is linguistically: moving water, and technically: covering the entire mouth with water. Indeed, istinshaq and madmadah are established by the narration of everyone who related the prophetic wudu from the Companions in the six authentic books and others. His saying: 'He only said: with waters' – the purpose is to justify the choice of the word 'with waters' over the word 'three times', because in choosing 'waters' there is an indication that the threefold repetition is with new water, based on the fact that 'waters' is a plural, and its minimum is three, and the individuals of the plural are diverse. If he had said 'three times', renewal of water would not be understood. His saying: 'to indicate renewal of water' – this is the sunnah according to us, and connecting is also permissible, as in al-Zahiriyyah. Likewise, the sunnah according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad is connecting, and separating is also permissible.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the rulings of madmadah (rinsing the mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water) in wudu. The author begins by stating the basis for these acts: the verbal hadiths encouraging siwak and the practical hadiths in the six authentic books. He notes that the time for siwak in wudu is at the moment of madmadah, and it is also recommended at every prayer. Regarding the method of using siwak, the recommended way is horizontally on the teeth and vertically on the tongue, according to the majority. He then addresses the permissibility of using someone else's siwak with permission, dismissing the notion of dislike. The commentary then focuses on the matn's wording 'with waters' (bimiyah) instead of 'three times' (thalathan). The author explains that this wording indicates the sunnah is to use fresh water for each of the three rinses and sniffs, because 'waters' implies multiple distinct portions. This contrasts with the Shafi'i school, where the sunnah is to combine the rinsing and sniffing using one handful of water for each set of three, i.e., without renewing the water for each. The Hanafi position is that the sunnah is to renew the water for each of the three times, but connecting the acts (without separation) is also permissible. The commentary thus clarifies an inter-madhhab dispute: the Hanafis consider renewal of water as sunnah, while the Shafi'is consider joining mouth and nose with one water as sunnah. Both schools allow the opposite as permissible but not the preferred practice.

Key Terms
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Linguistically: moving water; technically: covering the entire mouth with water.
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose, reaching the soft part (marin).
الوصل (al-wasl): Connecting the acts of madmadah and istinshaq by using one handful of water for both before renewing.
التثليث بمياه جديدة (al-tathlith bimiyah jadidah): Performing the threefold repetition with fresh water each time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Perform madmadah three times without renewing water, then istinshaq three times from the same water.
D) Alternate between mouth and nose with each of three handfuls.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the sunnah of madmadah and istinshaq is to use fresh water for each of the three times; connecting is permissible but not preferred.

Rinsing Mouth and Sniffing: Separate Handfuls vs. One Handful (Hanafi vs. Shafi'i)
Hanafi commentary explaining and defending the practice of separate water for madmadah and istinshaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on performing madmadah and istinshaq in wudu, identify the Shafi'i alternative, and evaluate the proofs for each view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi sunnah for wudu is to use six separate handfuls: three for the mouth and three for the nose.

The performance of madmadah (rinsing the mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water into the nose) in wudu involves a subtle difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. This passage clarifies the Hanafi view that each of the three repetitions should use fresh water for each act, totaling six handfuls, whereas the Shafi'i school combines both acts from a single handful per repetition. Understanding this distinction requires examining the hadith evidence and the interpretive principles that lead to different practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Madmadah and istinshaq are sunnah muakkadah in wudu. The specific method of repetition is also sunnah. Note that the text emphasizes the use of 'بمياه' (with waters) to indicate separate water.
The Hanafi school uses the plural form to stress renewal.
2
State the Hanafi position
The sunnah is to perform each of the three madmadahs and three istinshaqs with separate, fresh water. That is, three separate handfuls for madmadah and three separate handfuls for istinshaq, totaling six.
3
State the opposing school's position
The Shafi'i school holds that one should take a single handful, perform madmadah and istinshaq from it, and repeat twice more—so three handfuls total, each used for both actions.
This is referred to as 'wasl' (joining) in contrast to 'fasl' (separation).
4
Examine the evidence
The Shafi'is rely on hadiths in Bukhari and Muslim showing the Prophet performing both acts from one handful three times. The Hanafis rely on a hadith in Abu Dawud that the Prophet used to separate between the two, implying distinct water for each.
The Hanafi proof is a specific hadith of separation, considered by them to indicate the sunnah.
5
Analyze the tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred (mufta bihi) because the hadith of separation specifies a different practice, while the joining hadiths are interpreted as permissibility, not the primary sunnah.
The text asserts the Hanafi view as 'السنة' directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الوضوء بمياه، والاستنشاق بمياه) ، وإنما قال : بمياه ، ولم يقل : ثلاثاً ليدل على أنَّ المسنون التثليث بمياه جديدة، وإنَّما كرَّرَ قولَهُ بمياه ليدل على تجديد الماء لكل منهما خلافاً للشافعي ، فإنَّ المسنونَ عنده أن يمضمض ويستنشق بغرفة واحدة، ثم هكذا ثم هكذا.

واستدل القائلون بالوصل بما روى البخاري ومسلم من حديث عبد الله بن زيد ، وابن ماجه، وأبو داود من حديث ابن عباس ، والترمذي من حديث عبد الله ه ، والنسائي وابن ماجة من حديث علي : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مضمض واستنشق من غرفة واحدة ثلاث مرات». ودليلنا ما أخرجه أبو داود أنه كان يفصل بين المضمضة والاستنشاق.
Book of purification, wudu. 'With water, and the sniffling with water.' He only said 'with water' and did not say 'three times' to indicate that the sunnah is three times with new water, and he repeated his saying 'with water' to indicate renewing water for each of them, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for the sunnah according to him is to rinse the mouth and sniff from one handful, then like this, then like this. And those who say joining argued with what al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from the hadith of 'Abdullah ibn Zayd, and Ibn Majah and Abu Dawud from the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas, and al-Tirmidhi from the hadith of 'Abdullah, and al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah from the hadith of 'Ali: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) rinsed his mouth and sniffed from one handful three times.' And our proof is what Abu Dawud narrated that he used to separate between rinsing the mouth and sniffling.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The text begins by commenting on the matn phrase 'بمياه' in the context of madmadah (rinsing the mouth) and istinshaq (sniffing water into the nose) during wudu. The author explains that the Hanafi school considers it sunnah to perform each of these three times using separate, fresh water for each. The phrase 'بمياه' (with waters) is used instead of 'ثلاثاً' (three times) to emphasize that the three repetitions involve three different handfuls of water, not just three actions from a single handful. This is in contrast to the Shafi'i school, where the sunnah is to take one handful of water, rinse the mouth and sniff from it, and repeat that two more times—so all three madmadahs and istinshaqs are done from the same three handfuls, but each handful is used for both actions together. The text then presents the evidence of the opposing school (the 'qailun bil-wasl'): several hadiths from the Sahih collections show that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed madmadah and istinshaq from a single handful three times. The Hanafi response ('dililuna') is a hadith from Abu Dawud that the Prophet used to separate between madmadah and istinshaq, implying that each was done with its own water. The Hanafi position is that the sunnah is to use three separate handfuls for madmadah and three separate handfuls for istinshaq, making six total, although it is also permissible to join them by taking one handful for madmadah and one for istinshaq each time. The relied-upon view is the Hanafi position of separation, as it follows the explicit practice of the Prophet.

Key Terms
مَضْمَضَة (madmadah): Rinsing the mouth with water in wudu.
اسْتِنْشَاق (istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose and then expelling it.
غُرْفَة (ghurfah): A handful of water taken with cupped hands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Use one handful for madmadah and one for istinshaq, no repetition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah for madmadah and istinshaq is to use three separate handfuls for each, totaling six, based on the hadith of separation.

Khilal al-Lihyah: Sunnah or Permissible?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khilal al-lihyah: sunnah vs. ja'iz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on khilal al-lihyah, identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement, and explain why the sunnah view is preferred.

Core Idea
Combing the beard in wudu is sunnah — but that is the later relied-upon view; the early masters differed.

Combing the beard (khilal al-lihyah) during wudu is an act about which the earliest Hanafi imams disagreed. While Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani considered it merely permissible (ja'iz), Abu Yusuf held it to be a sunnah. The later school, guided by strong hadith evidence, adopted Abu Yusuf's view. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
State the act and the disputed ruling
Identify the act (khilal al-lihyah) and note that the hukm level is contested: sunnah vs. ja'iz.
Ensure you distinguish between the two levels: sunnah brings reward; ja'iz brings no reward nor sin.
2
Identify the disputants and their positions
Abu Yusuf holds sunnah; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold ja'iz. Note that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The disagreement is within the school, not between madhahib.
3
Analyze the evidence
The text cites hadith of 'Ammar, 'Uthman, Ibn 'Umar, Abu Ayyub, and Anas. Abu Yusuf's view is supported by these ahadith; the other two may require a firmer command to raise the level to sunnah.
In Hanafi usul, a sunnah is established by consistent practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) or a clear recommendation.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi scholars like the author of Manh al-Ghaffar and Ibn Abidin preferred Abu Yusuf's view, making khilal al-lihyah a sunnah in fatwa.
The mufta bihi is the operative ruling for practice.

Source Text

وتخليل اللحية وتخليل اللحية

And combing of the beard, and combing of the beard. His statement: 'And combing of the beard'; its manner according to the sunnah is that he insert the fingers of the hand into the gaps between the hairs from below upward such that the palm of the hand is outward and its back is toward the person performing wudu. Thus in 'Manh al-Ghaffar'. And it is recommended that it be with the right hand. And combing the beard has been established from the hadith of 'Ammar and 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them) in al-Tirmidhi, and from the hadith of Ibn 'Umar, Abu Ayyub, Anas and others in 'Manh al-Ghaffar'.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on khilal al-lihyah (combing the beard) during wudu. It first describes the recommended manner: inserting the fingers from underneath, with palm outward and back of hand toward the face, preferably with the right hand. Then it notes that the practice is established by hadiths. However, the passage does not explicitly state the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the commentary in the original text (as noted in the OCR footnotes) clarifies that Abu Yusuf considers it sunnah, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it merely permissible (ja'iz). The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf, as it is supported by the hadith evidence and preferred by later scholars like the author of 'Manh al-Ghaffar'. Thus, the Hanafi student should aim to perform khilal al-lihyah as a sunnah, though failure to do so does not invalidate the wudu.

Key Terms
تَخْلِيل اللِّحْيَة (khilal al-lihyah): Passing wet fingers through the beard during wudu to ensure water reaches the roots of the hair.

Key Takeaway

Combing the Beard: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on the ruling and method of combing the beard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on combing the beard during wudu, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and explain which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Combing the beard is sunnah for Abu Yusuf, but merely permissible for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; the relied-upon view is sunnah.

The commentary on combing the beard presents a clear method and strong hadith evidence. However, there is an internal Hanafi difference on whether it is a confirmed sunnah or just permissible. Understanding this distinction helps you know how much emphasis to place on this act in wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is khilal al-lihyah (combing the beard) during wudu. The dispute is whether it is sunnah (Abu Yusuf) or ja'iz (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view, making it a recommended sunnah.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
The text cites multiple hadith, especially from Anas that the Prophet said 'This is what my Lord commanded me.' This serves as the primary proof for the sunnah ruling.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad considered these hadith less strong, hence their view of ja'iz.
3
Understand the recommended method
The sunnah method is to insert fingers into the beard from below upward, palm outward, back of hand toward the person, preferably with the right hand.
This method is explained in Manh al-Ghaffar.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi tarjih
The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah favors Abu Yusuf based on the hadith evidence and the note from Manh al-Ghaffar, making the mufta bihi that it is sunnah.
This is a later tarjih; all three views are within the madhhab.
