Separating Madmada and Istinshaq: Hanafi Proof
Dispute on combining or separating madmada and istinshaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the Hanafi position on separating madmada and istinshaq from the opposing view, and identify the dalil for each.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school recommends separating rinsing the mouth and nose, based on a hadith that the Prophet separated them; combining is also valid but less virtuous.

The commentary briefly touches on a dispute regarding whether to combine or separate the rinsing of mouth and nose during wudu. The Hanafi school supports separation with a specific narration from Abu Dawud. This lesson examines the proofs and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
One position says to combine madmada and istinshaq with one handful of water three times. The other (Hanafi) says to separate them.
The text presents both, then gives the Hanafi proof.
2
Examine the proof for combining
Those who combine cite hadith from Bukhari, Muslim, and others that the Prophet used one handful three times for both.
These are authentic hadith, but the Hanafi school interprets them differently.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Abu Dawud narrated that the Prophet used to separate between rinsing the mouth and nose. This is the direct evidence for the Hanafi view.
The text says 'our proof' indicating the Hanafi school's preference.
4
Analyze the tarjih
The Hanafi school considers the separation hadith as more specific or as indicating the preferred method, while the combining hadith is interpreted as permissible but less optimal.
This is a standard Hanafi approach to harmonize conflicting hadith.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Practically, one may combine, but the sunnah is to separate. Wudu is valid in either case, but separation is more virtuous.
Thus, the hanafi position is a recommendation, not an obligation.

The Sunnah Method of Combing Fingers and Toes in Wudu
Explanation of the sunnah method and its proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the sunnah method for combing fingers and toes during wudu, state the ruling, and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Combing between fingers and toes is a sunnah of wudu with a specific method proven by the Prophet's practice.

This lesson explains the recommended (sunnah) act of combing between the fingers and toes during wudu. We will learn the exact method for hands and feet as transmitted in the Hanafi tradition, and the hadith evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Identify that the passage describes the sunnah of takhlil al-asabi'. The ruling is sunnah, not wajib or fard.
The ruling is based on the interpretation of the imperative in the hadith as istihbab.
2
Learn the method for hands
The method for hands is to interlace the fingers (tashbik al-asabi').
No specific order is mentioned for hands; interlacing suffices.
3
Learn the method for feet
For feet, use the little finger (khinsir) of the left hand, starting from the little toe of the right foot and ending with the little toe of the left foot.
This order is prescribed in the Hanafi sources.
4
Locate the hadith proof
The basis is the hadith narrated by the authors of the four Sunan: 'When you perform wudu, complete the wudu and comb between the fingers.' Also the specific narration from al-Mustawrid in Sunan Ibn Majah showing the Prophet combed his toes with his little finger.
The hadith is the primary dalil for this sunnah.
5
Understand the legal implication
The command 'khallil' indicates recommendation (istihbab) in Hanafi usul, not obligation, because the context of wudu and the absence of emphasis on obligation.
This is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that an imperative in acts of worship is for recommendation unless there is a qarinah indicating obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : والأصابع ؛ أي أصابع اليدين والرجلين، وكيفية تخليل أصابع اليدين أن يشبك الأصابع، والرجل أن يخلل بخنصر يده اليسرى ، باديـاً مـن خنصر رجلـه اليمني ، خاتماً بخنصر رجله اليسرى. كذا في «جامع المضمرات» وغيره. والأصل في هذا الباب حديث: «إذا توضأت فأسبغ الوضوء، وخلل بين الأصابع، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة، وفي سنن ابن ماجة» عن المستورد : «رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا توضأ ذلك أصابع رجليه بخنصره»
(r)
[1] His saying: 'And the fingers' means the fingers of the hands and the feet. And the method of combing the fingers of the hands is to interlace the fingers, and for the feet to use the little finger of the left hand, starting from the little toe of the right foot and ending with the little toe of the left foot. Thus in 'Jami' al-Mudmarat' and others. And the basis for this chapter is the hadith: 'When you perform wudu, complete the wudu and comb between the fingers,' narrated by the authors of the four Sunan. And in Sunan Ibn Majah from al-Mustawrid: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah ﷺ when he performed wudu, he combed his toes with his little finger.' (r)
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah of combing between the fingers and toes during wudu. The author clarifies that 'the fingers' refers to both hands and feet. For the hands, one interlaces the fingers. For the feet, one uses the little finger of the left hand, starting from the little toe of the right foot and ending with the little toe of the left foot. This method is derived from hadith, including the narration from al-Mustawrid who saw the Prophet combing his toes with his little finger. The ruling is that combing between the fingers is a sunnah, as it is part of completing the wudu.

Key Terms
تخليل الأصابع (takhlīl al-aṣābiʿ): The act of combing between the fingers and toes during wudu, specifically by interlocking for hands and using the little finger for toes.
الخنصر (al-khinṣir): The little finger, used for combing the toes in the prescribed sunnah method.

Washing Thrice in Wudu: A Confirmed Sunnah
Ruling on washing each limb three times in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on washing each limb thrice in wudu, identify the evidence from the hadith, and explain the exceptions due to necessity.

Core Idea
Washing thrice in wudu is a confirmed sunnah; leaving it without excuse is disliked.

This lesson examines the ruling of washing each limb three times during wudu, which the majority of scholars consider a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). We will explore the primary evidence from the hadith and the nuanced exceptions permitted under necessity, as well as the consequences of habitual omission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act in question is washing each limb three times (tathlith al-ghusl) during wudu. According to the Hanafi school, it is a sunnah mu'akkadah.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith narrated by Abu Dawud and others: the Prophet performed wudu three times each and said, 'This is the wudu. Whoever increases or decreases has done wrong and acted oppressively.'
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni in transmission.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's explicit statement that 'this is the wudu' indicates that thrice is the prescribed number. His warning against increasing or decreasing establishes the sunnah nature and discourages deviation without cause.
4
Apply the exception
From al-Binayah: if wudu is performed only once due to severe cold, lack of water, or other necessity, it is not disliked (makruh). This is an exception based on the principle of removing hardship.
The exception is not a license to habitually reduce.
5
Determine the ruling in other cases
If one washes once without any valid excuse, it is disliked (makruh). If a person habitually does so, some scholars consider it sinful (ithm). The default is to follow the sunnah of thrice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وتثليث الغسل ؛ كونه سنّة مؤكّدة ، هو ما عليه الأكثر، والأصل فيها ما روى أبو داود وغيره أنه لال له توضأ ثلاثاً ثلاثاً ، وقال : «هذا الوضوء، فمن زاد على هذا ونقص، فقد أساء وأظلم»(4). وذكر في «البناية» أنه «لو توضاً مرَّة مرَّة ؛ لقوة البرد أو لقلة الماء، أو لضرورة لا
يكره، وإلا يكره ، وقيل : إن اعتاد أثم، وإلا لا).
[2] His saying: 'And washing thrice'; that it is a confirmed sunnah is the view of the majority. The basis for this is what Abu Dawud and others narrated that he (the Prophet) said: 'Perform wudu three times three times, and said: This is the wudu. Whoever increases or decreases from this has done wrong and acted oppressively.' (4). And it is mentioned in al-Binayah: 'If he performs wudu once only due to severe cold, or lack of water, or necessity, it is not disliked. Otherwise, it is disliked. It is said: if he habitually does so, he sins; otherwise not.'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of washing each limb three times during wudu. The majority of scholars hold it to be a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). The primary proof is the hadith where the Prophet explicitly stated that wudu is to be done three times each, and warned against increasing or decreasing, calling it wrongdoing and oppression. However, the commentator also provides nuance from al-Binayah: if one washes only once due to severe cold, lack of water, or other necessity, it is not disliked. But if there is no excuse, it is disliked (makruh). If a person habitually washes only once, some say it becomes sinful. The default is that thrice is the sunnah, and reducing without valid reason is discouraged.

Key Terms
تثليث الغسل (tathlīth al-ghusl): Washing each limb three times in wudu, considered a confirmed sunnah.
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah mu'akkadah): A highly recommended act that the Prophet consistently performed and emphasized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to wash only once during wudu without it being disliked?
A) When in a hurry
B) When there is severe cold or lack of water
C) When one habitually does so
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Washing each limb thrice in wudu is a confirmed sunnah; leaving it without a valid excuse is disliked, and habitual neglect may be sinful.

Ruling on Wiping the Entire Head Once in Wudu
Inter-madhhab dispute with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on wiping the head in wudu and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Wiping the entire head once is obligatory; three wipes are not sunnah.

This passage presents the Hanafi position on wiping the head during wudu, contrasting with the Shafi'i school. Through authentic hadith evidence, the Hanafis establish that a single wipe covering the entire head is required. The passage also refutes the claim that wiping three times is sunnah, citing weak narrations and explaining the proper method.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the central ruling: wiping the entire head once is fard. Note that increasing beyond three wipes is considered bid'ah.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that wiping any part of the head suffices, and three wipes are sunnah.
The thamarah al-khilaf will show practical difference.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: hadith of Uthman wiping three times, and the principle that any part of the head satisfies the requirement.
The passage notes these hadith are weak.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how Hanafis rebut: authentic hadith (Ali, Abdullah ibn Zayd) show one wipe. Three-wipe narrations are weak or interpreted as three wipes with one water, or permissibility not obligation.
Hanafis also argue that three wipes, if habitual, become bid'ah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Clarify practical consequence: Hanafi wudu is invalid if any part of head is left unwiped; Shafi'i wudu valid with any touch. Three wipes are sunnah in Shafi'i but not in Hanafi.
This affects daily practice of millions.

ومسح كل الرأس مرة خلافاً للشافعي له. وذكر في «الخلاصة» و«التاتارخانية»: «إنَّ الزيادة على الثلاث بدعة». قوله: ومسح كل الرأس مرة؛ بثبوت ذلك بالروايات الصحيحة على ما مر. وكيفيته: أن يضع كفيه وأصابعه على مقدم رأسه ويمدهما إلى قفاه على وجه يستوعب جميع الرأس، ثم يمسح أذنيه بأصبعيه، ولا يكون الماء مستعملاً بهذا. كذا حققه الزيلعي في شرح الكنز، وصاحب «البحر»، و«النهر» و«فتح القدير» وغيرهم. وذكر في «المحيط» وغيره: «أنه يضع من كل واحدة من يديه ثلاث أصابع على مقدم رأسه، سوى الإبهام والسبابة، ويجافي بين كفيه ويمدهما إلى القفا، ثم يضع كفيه على مؤخّر رأسه، ويمدهما إلى مقدمه، ثم يمسح ظاهر كل إذن ظاهرة بإبهامه، وباطنه بمسبحة». فإن عنده تثليث المسح سُنَّة، وقد أوردَ التّرْمِذِي في «جامعه»: «أن علياً له توضأ فغسل أعضاءه ثلاثاً، ومسح رأسَهُ مرّةً واحدةً، وقال: هكذا وضوء رسول الله»، وفي «صحيح البخاري» مثل هذا. قوله: سنة؛ لما روي عن عثمان: «نه توضأ فمسح رأسه ثلاثاً، وقال: هكذا رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فعله» (أخرجه أبو داود والدارقطني والبزار والبيهقي وكذا رواه البزار من حديث عليّ)، وأسانيدها ضعيفة، وقد صرّح النووي وغيره: أن الأحاديث الصحاح على المسح مرّة. وحمل أصحابنا أحاديث التثليث على التثليث بماء واحد، والأولى حملها على بيان الجواز. قوله: إن عليّاً؛ ومثله روى أبو داود والنسائي، وفي «سنن ابن ماجة» عن عثمان وعلي وسلمة بن الأكوع قالوا: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح رأســه مـرة واحدة، وفي الباب أخبار أخر، بسطناها في «السعاية». قوله: مثل هذا؛ وهو من حديث عبد الله بن زيد المازني له في حكاية الوضوء النبوي.
And wiping the entire head once, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And it is mentioned in al-Khulasah and al-Tatarkhaniyyah: 'Indeed, increasing beyond three is innovation.' His saying: And wiping the entire head once; proven by authentic narrations as previously. Its method: he places his palms and fingers on the front of his head and extends them to the back of his neck in a way that encompasses the entire head, then he wipes his ears with his two fingers, and the water is not rendered used by this. Thus verified by al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz, and the author of al-Bahr, al-Nahr, Fath al-Qadir, and others. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit and others: 'He places from each of his hands three fingers on the front of his head, except the thumb and index finger, and he spreads apart his palms and extends them to the back of the neck, then he places his palms on the back of his head and extends them to the front, then he wipes the apparent of each apparent ear with his thumb, and its interior with his forefinger.' For according to him, wiping three times is sunnah, and al-Tirmidhi reported in his Jami': 'That Ali performed wudu and washed his limbs three times and wiped his head once, and said: This is the wudu of the Messenger of Allah,' and in Sahih al-Bukhari similar to this. His saying: sunnah; because it is narrated from Uthman: 'He performed wudu and wiped his head three times, and said: This is how I saw the Messenger of Allah do it' (recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Daraqutni, al-Bazzar, and al-Bayhaqi, and likewise al-Bazzar narrated from the hadith of Ali), and their chains are weak, and al-Nawawi and others stated: the authentic hadiths are on wiping once. And our companions interpreted the hadiths of three times as three times with one water, and it is more appropriate to interpret them as indicating permissibility. His saying: Indeed, Ali; and similar was narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i, and in Sunan Ibn Majah from Uthman, Ali, and Salamah ibn al-Akwa' that they said: the Messenger of Allah wiped his head once, and in this chapter are other reports, which we have expanded in al-Si'ayah. His saying: similar to this; and it is from the hadith of Abdullah ibn Zayd al-Mazini concerning the description of the prophetic wudu.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the obligation of wiping the entire head during wudu. The Hanafi school holds that wiping the entire head once is obligatory (fard), contrary to the Shafi'i school which considers wiping any part of the head sufficient. The author first states the ruling: 'wiping the entire head once,' and notes the disagreement with al-Shafi'i. Then he cites the Khulasah and Tatarkhaniyyah stating that increasing beyond three wipes is an innovation. The commentator explains that this ruling is proven by authentic hadith, such as those from Ali and Abdullah ibn Zayd, which show the Prophet wiping his head once. He then details the method: place the palms and fingers on the forehead, extend them to the nape, ensuring the entire head is covered, then wipe the ears. Alternate methods from al-Muhit involve using three fingers from each hand, excluding thumb and index. The passage then addresses the Shafi'i claim that wiping three times is sunnah. The commentator refutes this by noting that the hadiths supporting three wipes are weak, while the authentic hadiths clearly indicate once. The Hanafis interpret the three-wipe narrations as wiping three times with one application of water, or as demonstrating permissibility rather than obligation. The passage concludes with additional hadith references from Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah confirming the once-wipe practice.

Key Terms
مسح (mash): Wiping the head with wet hands during wudu; in this context, the entire head must be wiped once.
تثليث (tathlith): Wiping three times; considered by Shafi'i as sunnah, but Hanafi considers it bid'ah if done habitually.
بدعة (bid'ah): Innovation in religion; increasing beyond three wipes is considered blameworthy innovation.

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadith supporting three wipes are weak.
B) Because the Quran does not mention three wipes.
C) Because the Prophet never wiped three times.
D) Because the Hanafi school follows only one hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires wiping the entire head once in wudu, based on authentic hadith; three wipes are not sunnah and may be considered innovation.

Wiping the Ears with the Water of the Head
Expands matn ruling on wiping ears with head water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping the ears during wudu and explain the proof from the hadith and Prophetic practice.

Core Idea
The ears are wiped with the water of the head—no fresh water required, based on the hadith and practice of the Prophet.

In this passage, the commentator explains the matn statement that the ears are wiped with the water of the head. This ruling is based on clear sunnah evidence and distinguishes the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i school. We will examine the proof and the recommended practice of renewing water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is wiping the ears during wudu. The ruling is that it is wajib to wipe the ears with the water of the head, not with fresh water.
The matn says 'and the ears with its water' meaning the water of the head.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith 'The ears are from the head' (Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, et al.) and the recorded practice of the Prophet ﷺ wiping his head and ears without taking new water.
These proofs are from Sunnah ahad but have a good chain and support the Hanafi view.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit statement 'The ears are from the head' indicates legal inclusion; the Prophet's action demonstrates that the ears are wiped with the same water as the head.
The hadith is used to establish that the ears share the same water, not just anatomical origin.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Who disagrees and why? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the ears are a separate organ requiring fresh water, based on a different interpretation of the hadith or qiyas.
The disagreement does not affect the Hanafi reliance on the hadith.
5
Evaluate the nuance on renewing water
What about taking fresh water specifically for the ears? The passage concludes that renewing water is sunnah (recommended) but not wajib, based on some narrations and the preference of the commentator.
This is a intra-Hanafi nuance: Abu Hanifa did not recommend it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad considered it sunnah.

(And the ears with its water): i.e., with the water of the head, contrary to him. [1] His saying: 'and the ears' is conjuncted to 'all of the head'. Its manner as in al-Mujtaba and al-Binaya: 'he wipes their insides with his index fingers and their outsides with his thumbs', and this is established from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ in Sunan Ibn Majah. And it is said: he inserts his little finger into the ear canals. [2] His saying: 'with its water'; i.e., with water taken for wiping the head, due to the hadith: 'The ears are from the head' reported by Ibn Majah, al-Daraqutni, al-Tabarani, Abu Dawud, and al-Tirmidhi with a good chain, and it is established in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar of al-Tahawi, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan Abi Dawud, and others: 'The Prophet ﷺ wiped his head and ears without taking new water.' (Footnote: with various wordings from Ibn Abbas: '...then he cupped a handful of water, wiped his head and ears, inner with index fingers, outer with thumbs'; from Uthman ibn Affan: '...he wiped his head and ears, outer and inner, and said: I saw the Prophet ﷺ doing this' ... al-Tahawi said: 'These narrations indicate that the ruling of the ears is like that of the head for wiping, and this has been transmitted in tawatur ... and from rational proof: the woman in ihram is not allowed to cover her face but is allowed to cover her ears, which shows they are like the head.') So renewing water for wiping the ears is sunnah according to him.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn 'والأذنين بمائه' meaning the ears are wiped with the water of the head. The commentator explains that 'its water' refers to the water used for wiping the head, not new water. This is contrary to the view of Imam Shafi'i who holds that the ears are a separate part requiring fresh water. The commentator then quotes two hadiths: 'The ears are from the head' (reported with a good chain) and the actual practice of the Prophet ﷺ wiping his head and ears together without taking new water. A long footnote by al-Tahawi provides multiple narrations and a rational argument: a woman in ihram cannot cover her face but can cover her ears, proving the ears have the same ruling as the head. The passage concludes that renewing water for the ears is sunnah according to the author's school (likely referring to Abu Hanifa or the relied-upon view).

Key Terms
ماء الرأس (ma' al-ra's): The water that is taken for wiping the head (i.e., from the same handful or cupping of water used for the head)
الأذنان من الرأس (al-udhunan min al-ra's): Hadith meaning the ears are legally considered part of the head for purposes of wudu
تجديد الماء (tajdid al-ma'): Taking fresh water for wiping the ears, separate from the water used for the head

The ears are wiped with the water of the head—this is the Hanafi position based on the hadith 'الأذنان من الرأس' and the practice of the Prophet ﷺ; renewing water is sunnah, not wajib.

The Meaning of 'Order Which is Stated in the Text' in Wudu
Commentary clarifying the meaning of 'nass' in the matn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Quranic mention of wudu order and the technical usul term 'nass', and explain why intention is not derived from the Quran according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The order of wudu is mentioned in the Quran, but intention is not — and 'nass' here means 'mentioned', not 'explicitly intended'.

The matn of al-Wiqayah says: 'intention and the order which is stated in the text'. The commentator clarifies that this phrase refers only to the order (al-tartib) being mentioned in the Quran, not to intention. He then defends the Hanafi position that intention is not derived from the Quran, rebutting a Shafi'i argument. He also distinguishes between 'nass' as a simple mention and the technical usul term for explicit primary intent. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and its implications for the Hanafi understanding of wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn phrase and its elements
Look at the matn: 'والنية، والترتيب الذي نص عليه'. The commentator states that 'الذي نص عليه' is a description of 'الترتيب' only, not of 'النية'. Understand that intention is not mentioned in the Quran.
The matn combines two elements, but the commentator separates their textual basis.
2
Analyze the Shafi'i argument for deriving intention
Some Shafi'is argue that the conditional clause 'إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصَّلَوَةِ فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ' implies that washing is for the sake of prayer, which is intention. The commentator refutes this: the condition only requires the existence of the act (standing for prayer), not the intention behind it.
The Hanafi response is that conditions are observed by their existence, not by intent. This is a key usul principle.
3
Distinguish between 'nass' as mention and 'nass' as technical term
The commentator clarifies that here 'نص' means 'mentioned in the Quran', not the technical usul term for speech explicitly intended for its subject. He rebuts three potential misunderstandings: (1) that it means explicitly stated in books, (2) that it implies the order is commanded as a primary intent, (3) that it is the technical 'mansus'.
The verse's purpose is to list the pillars of wudu, not to legislate the order. The order is mentioned but not commanded as a primary intent.
4
Apply the Hanafi position on the source of order and intention
From this passage, the Hanafi position is: the order of wudu limbs is wajib based on the Quranic mention (via the 'fa' of sequence in verse 5:6), while intention is a condition (shart) for wudu but is established only from the Sunnah, not the Quran.
This distinction affects the epistemological weight and the ruling on missing intention or order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والنية، والترتيب الذي نص عليه ) : أي الترتيب المذكور في نص القرآن
[١] قوله : سنة عنده ؛ لما أخرجه الحاكم في مستدركه، والبيهقي وصححه عن عبد الله بن زيد : أنه رأى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يتوضأ، فأخذ لأذنيه ماءً خلاف الماء الذي أخذ لرأسه، وروى نحوه مالك من فعل ابن عمر وأجاب عنه أصحابنا بحمله على بيان الجواز، وبأنَّ التجديد إذا لم تبق في اليد تلة.
[٢] قوله : أي الترتيب ؛ أشار بهذا إلى أن قوله الذي نص عليه وصف الترتيب فقط لا لكل واحد من الترتيب. أو النية فإنّ النيَّة لا ذكر لها في القرآن، ومن الشافعية من أخذها من قوله : إِذَا قُمْتُمْ إِلَى الصَّلَوَةِ فَأَغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ الآية ، بأنّ حكم الغسل خرج مخرج جزاء الشرط ، فتقديره : فاغسلوا وجوهكم ... الخ ؛ للقيام إلى الصلاة، وهذا هو النية، وفيه نظر، فإن شأن الشرط أن يراعى وجوداً لا وجوده قصداً.
[٣] قوله : المذكور في نص القرآن ؛ أشار بهذا إلى أنه ليس المراد من التصريح في الكتب كما يقال : هذه المسألة منصوصة أي مصرحة في الكتب، بل المراد به المذكور في القرآن. وأيضاً أشار إلى دفع ما يتوهم أنه لما كان الترتيب منصوصاً في القرآن، فكيف جاز خلافه، بأنه ليس معناه أنه منصوص بأمره في القرآن، بل المراد المذكور فيه. وأيضاً أشار إلى دفع ما يتوهم من أنّ النصوص في اصطلاح أهل الأصول عبارة عما سيق الكلام لأجله وقصد به ، وما لم يسق له الكلام، ويفهم من فحواه يقال له : الظاهر، ومن المعلوم أن سوق الآية ليس لبيان الترتيب بل لمجرد بيان أركان الوضوء، بأنه ليس المراد به المنصوص المصطلح بل المذكور.
(١) في سنن البيهقي الكبير (١ : ٦٥) ، ومعرفة السنن والآثار (١ : ٢٢٩)، وغيرها.
(٢) المائدة : من الآية ٦ .
(And intention, and the order which is stated in the text): i.e., the order mentioned in the text of the Quran. [1] His saying: 'sunnah according to him' because of what al-Hakim and al-Bayhaqi authenticated from Abdullah ibn Zayd: that he saw the Prophet ﷺ performing wudu, and he took for his ears water different from the water he took for his head, and Malik related something similar from Ibn Umar. Our companions answered by interpreting it as showing permissibility, and that renewal [applies] when no wetness remains in the hand. [2] His saying: 'i.e., the order' indicates that his saying 'which is stated in the text' is a description of the order only, not of each one of order or intention, because intention is not mentioned in the Quran. Some Shafi'is derive it from His saying 'When you stand up for prayer, wash your faces...' claiming that the ruling of washing comes as a conditional clause, so its appreciation is: 'wash your faces... for standing up to prayer', and this is intention. But this is questionable, because the characteristic of a condition is that its existence is considered, not its intention. [3] His saying: 'mentioned in the text of the Quran' indicates that what is meant is not 'explicitly stated in the books' as it is said 'this issue is mansus i.e., explicitly stated in the books'; rather, what is meant is 'mentioned in the Quran'. And it also indicates rebutting the assumption that since order is explicitly commanded in the Quran, how could its contrary be allowed? It is not meant that it is explicitly commanded; rather, it is mentioned. And it also rebuts the assumption that 'nusus' in the terminology of usul scholars refers to what the speech is intended for and aimed at, while what is not intended but understood from context is called 'zahir'. It is known that the context of the verse is not to explain order but only to explain the pillars of wudu, so the intended meaning is not the technical 'mansus' but 'mentioned'.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn 'والنية، والترتيب الذي نص عليه'. The commentator first clarifies a related point about renewing water for ears (a continuation from the previous section) but then focuses on the meaning of 'الذي نص عليه'. He explains that 'which is stated in the text' is a description of 'al-tartib' (order) only, not of 'al-niyyah' (intention), since intention is not mentioned in the Quran. He criticizes the Shafi'i argument that intention is derived from the conditional form of the verse (Quran 5:6). Then he defines 'nass' here as 'mentioned in the Quran', distinguishing it from the technical usul term 'mansus' (explicitly intended). This addresses three potential misunderstandings: (1) that 'nass' means explicit in books, (2) that if order is commanded in the Quran, violating it would be impossible, and (3) that 'nass' in usul means the primary intent whereas the verse's intent is not to prescribe order but to list pillars. The passage thus clarifies the textual basis for the wudu order: it is mentioned in the Quran but not as a primary command.

Key Terms
نص عليه (nassa 'alayhi): Mentioned in the text of the Quran; referring to the fact that the order of wudu limbs is mentioned in the verse (5:6)
النية (al-niyyah): Intention of the heart performed before or at the start of wudu
الترتيب (al-tartib): The prescribed order of washing limbs in wudu: face, arms, head, feet

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi understanding in this passage, why is the word 'نص' in 'الذي نص عليه' not the technical usul term?
A) Because the verse is not primarily intended to command order.
B) Because the verse is ambiguous.
C) Because the order is only recommended, not obligatory.
D) Because the term 'نص' in usul means only Quranic text.

Key Takeaway
The order of wudu limbs is mentioned in the Quran, but intention is not; the term 'nass' here indicates mere mention, not technical explicit command.

Taharah — Purification

Intention and Order in Wudu: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute over whether intention and order are fard in wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that intention and order are not fard in wudu and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi al-niyyat'.

Core Idea
Intention and order in wudu are sunnah, not fard; prayer without them is valid in the Hanafi school.

This passage confronts a classic inter-madhhab dispute: are intention (niyyah) and order (tartib) obligatory (fard) in wudu? The Shafi'i school holds that they are, citing the well-known hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi al-niyyat' and the order of actions in the Quran. The Hanafi school disagrees, interpreting the evidence differently. In this lesson, you will trace the arguments and understand the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly articulate the Hanafi school's view: intention and order are not fard; wudu without them is valid for prayer, though not complete.
The Hanafi school considers these as sunnah muakkadah.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Name the opposing school (Shafi'i) and state that they consider both intention and order as fard (obligatory), invalidating prayer if omitted.
Imam al-Shafi'i is the primary opponent.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the verse 'When you rise to prayer...' is taken to imply intention, and the hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi al-niyyat' appears to require intention for every action's validity.
The Shafi'is also note the sequential order in the verse as evidence for tartib.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafis rebut: the verse refers to the action of washing, not to intending it; the condition is fulfilled by existence, not intention. The hadith's apparent generality is abandoned because many actions exist without intention; thus it negates perfection, not validity.
This is supported by the principle that 'a condition is fulfilled by its existence'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: a Hanafi's prayer is valid even if wudu was without intention or out of order, while a Shafi'i must repeat wudu to make it valid.
The difference affects daily practice, especially for forgetful persons.

[1] His statement: fard; such that if one performed wudu without order or without intention, prayer with that wudu would not be valid. And according to us (Hanafis), prayer with it is valid, even if the commanded wudu is not performed, and reward does not result from it. He said in 'Jami' al-Mudmarat': The place of disagreement is that if the one performing wudu forgot to wipe his head and then rain hit him, it suffices according to us. So the result is that intention is a condition in wudu that is a means of approach and worship by consensus. The disagreement is only whether wudu that is not intended is a key to prayer or not. And taking it to negation of validity is preferable because it is closer to negation of the thing itself. And it is known that the definite article 'al-' on 'actions' is for generality, indicating that intention is required for the validity of every action, whether it is from means or from ends. Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning two elements of wudu: intention (niyyah) and order (tartib). The Shafi'i school considers both obligatory (fard), while the Hanafi school does not. The commentary begins by explaining the statement 'and intention, and the order that is stated' from the base text. It clarifies that 'that which is stated' refers to the order mentioned in the Quran (Quran 5:6), but not that the Quran explicitly commands it—rather it is merely mentioned. It then addresses the Shafi'i argument that intention is derived from the verse 'When you rise to prayer, wash your faces...' by considering the phrase 'when you rise' as a condition implying the intention to pray. The Hanafi commentator refutes this, noting that a condition is fulfilled by its mere occurrence, not by consciously intending it. Next, the passage states that according to al-Shafi'i, both intention and order are fard, to the extent that wudu performed without order or without intention invalidates prayer. In contrast, the Hanafi position (indicated by 'at us') is that prayer with such wudu is valid, though the commanded wudu is not fully achieved and reward is absent. The commentator cites 'Jami' al-Mudmarat' to clarify that the disagreement is not about intention being a condition for wudu as an act of worship—that is agreed—but whether wudu without intention renders the prayer invalid. Finally, the famous hadith 'Indeed actions are by intentions' is discussed. The Shafi'is use it to require intention for validity. The Hanafi response is that the hadith's apparent generality is not applicable (it is 'matruk al-zahir' because many actions occur without intention), so it must be interpreted as negating the ideal ruling (perfection) rather than validity. The passage then lists various narrations of the hadith from the major hadith collections.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): The intention in the heart to perform an act of worship; in wudu, it is the aim to perform wudu for the purpose of prayer or worship.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Performing the actions of wudu in the sequence specified in the Quran: face, hands, head, feet.
فرض (fard): An obligation whose omission invalidates the act and requires repetition; according to the Shafi'i school in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on performing wudu without intention (niyyah)?
A) The wudu is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The wudu is valid but incomplete; prayer performed with it is valid.
C) The wudu is invalid and prayer cannot be performed with it.
D) The wudu is valid only if the person is in a state of ritual impurity.

Key Takeaway
Intention and order are not fard in Hanafi wudu; the prayer is valid without them, though the wudu is incomplete.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

The Role of Intention in Wudu: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on whether intention (niyyah) is a condition for wudu's validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the role of intention (niyyah) in wudu, compare it with the Shafi'i position, and evaluate the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof from hadith.

Core Idea
Intention (niyyah) is required for reward in wudu, not for its validity; the Hadith ‘إنما الأعمال بالنيات’ is about reward, not validity.

The requirement of intention (niyyah) in wudu is a point of significant disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While both schools agree that intention is essential for earning reward, they diverge on whether intention is a condition for the validity of wudu itself. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the Shafi'i view that both order (tartib) and intention are obligatory components (fard) of wudu, and then provides the Hanafi refutation based on the interpretation of the famous hadith 'Indeed actions are by intentions.' We will analyze the key proof, the Hanafi response, and the practical consequences of this dispute for daily worship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on intention in wudu
Identify what the Hanafi school rules regarding wudu performed without intention. Is it valid? Does it require compensation?
The Hanafi position is that wudu without niyyah is valid for salah but no reward is earned.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Determine which school (or scholar) disagrees and what they hold regarding intention in wudu. Note the specific ruling they assign to intention.
Imam al-Shafi'i considers intention to be a fard (obligatory component) of wudu, without which wudu is invalid.
3
Follow the opponent's proof from hadith
Examine the Shafi'i use of the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' as evidence. How do they derive the necessity of intention for validity from this hadith?
The Shafi'is rely on the apparent generality of the hadith, arguing that the lam in 'al-a'mal' indicates that every action requires intention for validity.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school rebuts the Shafi'i proof. What interpretation of the hadith do they offer, and what consensus do they cite?
The Hanafis argue that the hadith is agreed to be about reward, not validity. If 'ruling' is assumed, it includes both worldly (validity) and otherworldly (reward), but the otherworldly is the intended meaning by consensus.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Identify the real-world difference between the two positions. How does this affect the validity of salah performed with such wudu?
Hanafis: wudu without niyyah is valid, so salah is valid. Shafi'is: wudu without niyyah is invalid, so salah is also invalid.

And both are fard according to him [al-Shafi'i]. As for intention (niyyah), it is due to his statement: 'Indeed actions are by intentions.' [1] His statement: 'fard'; so if one performs wudu without order or without intention, prayer with that wudu is not permissible. And according to us, prayer is permissible with it, even if the commanded wudu is not fulfilled and reward does not result from it. He said in Jami' al-Mudmarat: 'The point of dispute is that if a person performing wudu forgets to wipe his head and then rain falls on it, it suffices according to us. So the conclusion is that intention is a condition in wudu that is a devotional act by agreement. The dispute is only whether wudu that is not intended is a key to prayer or not.' [2] His statement: 'due to his statement'; the explanation of the argument by it according to the Shafi'i books is that the apparent meaning of the hadith necessitates that no action exists without intention, but that is not so, for many actions exist without intention and deliberation. Therefore, it must be that its apparent meaning is abandoned, so the intended meaning is the negation of the ruling of actions without intention, such as validity or perfection. And interpreting it as negation of validity is more appropriate because it resembles the negation of the thing itself. And it is known that the lam (definite article) in 'al-a'mal' is for generalization, so it indicates the conditionality of intention for the validity of every action, whether from the means or the ends. [3] His statement: 'Indeed actions are by intentions' (1); this hadith was narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta, and the authors of the six books and others through multiple chains with different wordings. In the chapter of the Beginning of Revelation in Sahih al-Bukhari, it is worded: 'Indeed actions are by intentions'; in the Book of Nikah, worded: 'The action is by intention'; in the Book of Manumission, worded: 'Actions are by intention'; similarly in the Book of Hijra; in the Book of Oaths, worded: 'Indeed actions are by intention'; similarly in the Book of Legal Stratagems. And in Sahih Ibn Hibban, worded: 'Actions are by intentions.' Al-Nawawi mentioned in Bustan al-Arifin that Abu Musa al-Asbahani narrated it with the wording: 'Indeed actions are by intentions.' And Ibn al-Humam mentioned that Ibn al-Jarud included it in al-Muntaqa with the wording: 'Indeed actions are by intention.' [1] His statement: 'Indeed reward...' etc. Its gist is that obtaining reward in acts of worship is contingent upon intention by agreement. So if actions are devoid of the intention of obedience and desire to draw near to Allah, reward is not obtained, whether they are from the means like wudu and tayammum or pure acts of worship. So it is necessary to assume that reward is omitted in this hadith, and it is said: its meaning is 'Reward of actions is only by intention.' Or something that includes reward and other things is omitted, such as 'ruling' (hukm), for it includes reward (the otherworldly ruling) and validity (the worldly ruling), and it is said: its meaning is 'Indeed the ruling of actions is by intentions.' If reward is assumed, it is obvious that the hadith does not indicate the conditionality of intention for the validity of acts of worship, but rather indicates its conditionality for obtaining reward, which is contrary to what al-Shafi'i intended and exactly what we intended. And if 'ruling' is assumed, then although in its apparent meaning it indicates the dependence of validity on intention, yet we say: Ruling is of two types: worldly: validity and invalidity; and otherworldly: reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the role of intention (niyyah) in wudu. The Shafi'i school maintains that intention is a fard (obligatory component) of wudu, and without it, wudu is invalid and prayer cannot be performed with it. The Hanafi school, however, holds that intention is not a condition for the validity (sihha) of wudu, but rather for the reward (thawab). The text begins by stating the Shafi'i position: both tartib (order) and niyyah are fard. The commentary then explains the Hanafi view: if one performs wudu without order or intention, prayer is still valid according to the Hanafis, though the commanded wudu is not fulfilled and no reward is earned. An illustrative example from Jami' al-Mudmarat is given: a person forgets to wipe his head, but rain wets it; according to the Hanafi school, this suffices for the wiping, showing that the absence of intention (since the rain was not intended as an act of wiping) does not invalidate the wudu. The passage then discusses the hadith 'Indeed actions are by intentions,' which the Shafi'is use as proof that intention is required for the validity of every action. The Hanafi response is that the hadith is understood by all to relate to reward, not validity. They argue that the apparent generality of the hadith is not applicable here because the context (agreed upon) is about reward. Alternatively, if 'ruling' is intended, ruling includes both worldly (validity) and otherworldly (reward), and the otherworldly reward is the intended meaning by consensus. Thus, the hadith does not prove that intention is a condition for the validity of wudu. The dispute is ultimately about whether wudu without niyyah can be a key to prayer—Hanafis say yes, Shafi'is say no.

Key Terms
نِيَّة (niyyah): Intention or purpose to perform an act of worship. In this passage, the dispute is whether niyyah is required for the validity of wudu or only for reward.
فَرْض (fard): An obligatory component of an act. In this passage, al-Shafi'i considers both tartib (order) and niyyah as fard in wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on wudu performed without intention (niyyah)?
A) The wudu is invalid and prayer cannot be performed with it.
B) The wudu is valid for prayer but no reward is earned.
C) The wudu is valid and full reward is earned.
D) The wudu is void and must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that intention is a condition for reward in wudu but not for its validity, based on the interpretation of the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' as referring to reward, not validity.

Hanafi Response on Intention: Reward vs. Validity
Hanafi rebuttal to Shafi'i interpretation of niyyah hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the role of niyyah in wudu and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Niyyah is required for reward, not for validity—a key Hanafi distinction.

The hadith 'Deeds are by intentions' is often cited to argue that intention is mandatory for the validity of acts like wudu. The Hanafi school, however, interprets this hadith differently: it requires intention for divine reward, not for the legal validity of the act. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i position, using the concepts of worldly and otherworldly rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on niyyah in wudu
Identify that Hanafis consider niyyah necessary for reward (thawab) but not for the validity (sihha) of wudu.
This is the core distinction.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that niyyah is a condition (shart) for the validity of wudu, based on the general wording of the hadith.
3
Examine the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'Innama al-a'malu bi al-niyyat' (Deeds are by intentions) to argue that intention must be present for the act to be valid.
The hadith is authentic but its interpretation is contested.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi commentator argues that the hadith speaks about reward, not validity. 'Ruling' (hukm) can be divided into worldly (validity) and otherworldly (reward). Since consensus affirms that reward depends on intention, the hadith is restricted to the otherworldly sense.
The consensus (ijma) is key: all agree reward requires niyyah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if wudu is performed without niyyah, it is valid according to Hanafis (but no reward) and invalid according to Shafi'is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابنا: إن الثواب منوط بالنية اتفاقاً، فلا بد أن يقدر الثواب، أو يقدر شيء يشمل الثواب، نحو: حكم الأعمال بالنيات، فإن قدر الثواب فظاهر، وإن قدر الحكم، فهو نوعان: دنيوي كالصحة، وأخروي كالثواب، والأخروي مراد بالإجماع. رواه أبو موسى الأصبهاني بلفظ: «إن الأعمال بالنيات»، وذكر ابن الهمام أن ابن الجارود أخرجه في «المنتقى» بلفظ: «إن الأعمال بالنية». قوله: إن الثواب... الخ، حاصله أن حصول الثواب في العبادات موقوف على النية اتفاقاً، حتى أن الأعمال إذا خلت عن قصد الطاعة وإرادة التقرب إلى الله لا يحصل ثوابها، سواء كان من قبل الوسائل، كالوضوء والتيمم، أو العبادات المحضة، فلا بد أن يحذف الثواب في هذا الحديث، ويقال: معناه ثواب الأعمال ليس إلا بالنية. أو يحذف شيء يشمل الثواب وغيره: كالحكم، فإنه يشمل الثواب، وهو الحكم الأخروي، والصحة وهو الحكم الدنيوي، ويقال: معناه إنما حكم الأعمال بالنيات، فإن قدر الثواب فظاهر أنه لا دلالة للحديث المذكور على اشتراط النية لصحة العبادات، بل إنما يدل على اشتراطها لحصول الثواب، وهو خلاف ما أراده الشافعي، وعين ما أردناه. وإن قدر الحكم فهو وإن كان بظاهره يفيد توقف الصحة على النية، إلا أنا نقول: الحكم نوعان: دنيوي: وهو الصحة والفساد. وأخروي: كالثواب والعقاب. والأخروي مراد في هذا الحديث بالإجماع، فإنهم أجمعوا على أنه لا ثواب إلا بالنية. فإذا قيل: حكم الأعمال بالنيات وأريد به الثواب صح هذا الكلام من غير ضرورة إلى أن يحمل على العموم، ويجعل شاملاً للصحة، فلا يحمل الحكم على المعنى الأعم؛ لأن ما ثبت بالضرورة يتقدر بقدر الضرورة، والاحتياج إلى حذف الثواب أو ما يعمه إنما وقع لعدم استقامة ظاهر الحديث المقتضي لنفي وجود الأعمال بدون النية، فلما اندفع ذلك بإرادة الثواب، لا يراد غيره. فاحفظ هذا كله، ولا تنظر إلى تقريرات الناظرين كأخي جلبي، والسيد مهدي، وعبد الله الهروي وغيرهم، فإنهم قرروا كلام الشارح هاهنا بما لا ينبغي كما فصلنا ذلك في «السعاية».
Our answer: Verily, reward is dependent upon intention by consensus. So it is necessary to estimate [the meaning of] reward, or to estimate something that includes reward, like: 'the ruling of deeds depends on intentions.' If reward is intended, it is apparent; if ruling is intended, it is of two types: worldly like validity, and otherworldly like reward, and the otherworldly is meant by consensus. Narrated by Abu Musa al-Isbahani with the wording: 'Verily deeds are by intentions,' and Ibn al-Humam mentioned that Ibn al-Jarud narrated it in al-Muntaqa with the wording: 'Verily deeds are by intention.' His statement: 'Verily reward...' etc., its summary is that obtaining reward in acts of worship is conditional upon intention by consensus, such that if deeds are devoid of the intent of obedience and desire to draw near to Allah, they do not attain reward, whether they are of the type of means like wudu and tayammum, or pure acts of worship. So it is necessary to delete [the word] reward in this hadith and say: its meaning is 'the reward of deeds is only by intention,' or to delete something that includes reward and others: like 'ruling,' for it includes reward (the otherworldly ruling) and validity (the worldly ruling), and say: its meaning is 'indeed the ruling of deeds is by intentions.' If reward is intended, it is apparent that the aforementioned hadith does not indicate the condition of intention for the validity of acts of worship; rather it only indicates its condition for attaining reward, and this is the opposite of what al-Shafi'i intended and exactly what we intended. And if ruling is intended, even though its apparent meaning suggests the dependence of validity on intention, yet we say: ruling is of two types: worldly: which is validity and invalidity, and otherworldly: like reward and punishment. And the otherworldly is meant in this hadith by consensus. For they have agreed that there is no reward except with intention. If it is said: 'the ruling of deeds is by intentions' and reward is intended, this statement is valid without necessitating that it be taken as general and made to include validity. So the ruling is not taken in the general sense, because what is established by necessity is limited to the extent of necessity. The need to delete 'reward' or what includes it only arose due to the lack of directness of the apparent meaning of the hadith which entails the negation of the existence of deeds without intention. When that was repelled by intending reward, nothing else is intended. So memorize all this, and do not look at the expositions of later scholars like Akhi Jalabi, al-Sayyid Mahdi, and Abd Allah al-Harawi, for they have explained the words of the commentator here in an inappropriate manner, as we have detailed in al-Si'aya.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi response to the Shafi'i school's use of the hadith 'Indeed deeds are by intentions' to argue that intention is obligatory for the validity of acts of worship (like wudu). The Hanafi commentator begins by stating that reward (thawab) is dependent on intention by consensus. He then argues that the hadith must be interpreted as referring to reward, not validity. He demonstrates that the word 'ruling' (hukm) can be divided into worldly (validity, nullity) and otherworldly (reward, punishment). Since the hadith is used to prove that reward requires intention by consensus, it does not necessarily entail that validity also requires intention. The commentator insists that the intended meaning in this hadith is the otherworldly ruling (reward) by consensus. He warns against misinterpreting the hadith to imply that intention is needed for validity, which is the Shafi'i view. The passage ends by cautioning against the expositions of certain later scholars who misunderstood the commentator's words.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention; the mental resolve to perform an act of worship for the sake of Allah.
ثواب (thawab): Divine reward; recompense in the Hereafter for an act of obedience.
حكم (hukm): Ruling; here it is divided into worldly (validity/invalidity) and otherworldly (reward/punishment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To require intention for the reward of deeds
C) To forbid actions without intention
D) To make intention a pillar of wudu

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that niyyah is necessary for reward, not for validity, based on a distinction between worldly and otherworldly rulings.

Intention and Reward: Three Objections Refuted
Critical examinations of objections to the Hanafi position on niyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three objections to the Hanafi position on niyyah and reward, and explain the commentator's responses.

Core Idea
Reward requires intention—the consensus of early Hanafis overrides later weak opinions.

The Hanafi school holds that intention is required for reward in acts of worship. However, some later Hanafis questioned this, and three logical objections were raised against the consensus claim. In this passage, the commentator systematically refutes each objection, reinforcing the majority position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Find the first investigation: it challenges the claim of consensus by citing a disagreement among Hanafis recorded in al-Muhit and Khizanat al-Muftin.
Some later scholars said reward could be obtained without intention.
2
Understand the commentator's response
The commentator dismisses the opposing view as weak and reasserts the consensus of the majority of early scholars.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second argument suggests that the hadith linking intention to reward might not be the sole proof; the commentator insists it is the primary evidence, making the inference valid.
4
Examine the third objection
The third claims that 'deeds' in the hadith might be metaphorical, avoiding the need for estimation. The commentator implies this still supports his interpretation.
5
Synthesize the overall position
The mufta bihi is that intention is required for reward; the minority view is weak. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المقام أبحاث: الأول: إن دعوى كون الثواب منوطاً بالنية اتفاقاً ممنوعة، فقد ذكر في «المحيط» و«خزانة المفتين»: إنهم تكلموا في أنه إذا ترك النية هل يجد ثواب الوضوء أم لا؟ فقال أكثر المتقدمين: لا يثاب، وقال بعض المتأخرين: يثاب. والجواب عنه: إن القول بحصول الثواب بدون النية لما كان ضعيفاً لم يعتبر به، وادعى بالاتفاق. والثاني: إن كون الثواب منوطاً بالنية لا يوجب أن يقدر الثواب، أو ما يشمله في الحديث المذكور، بل يجوز أن يكون ذلك ثابتاً بدليل آخر. والجواب عنه: إن معنى قوله: «إن الثواب منوطاً بالنية» أن الثواب موقوف على النية بهذا الحديث؛ لأنهم يستدلون عليه بهذا الحديث، وحينئذ لا ريب في صحة تفريعه بقوله: «فلا بد أن يقدر ...» الخ. والثالث: إن التقدير ليس بلازم، لجواز أن تكون الأعمال مجازاً عن حكمها، فلا يحتاج إلى حذف شيء.
And in this place there are investigations: First: The claim that reward is dependent on intention by consensus is objectionable, for it is mentioned in al-Muhit and Khizanat al-Muftin: They discussed whether if one omits intention he obtains reward for wudu or not. The majority of the early scholars said: he does not obtain reward, and some later scholars said: he does obtain reward. The answer to it: The statement that reward is obtained without intention, since it is weak, is not considered, and [a claim of] consensus is made. Second: That the dependence of reward on intention does not necessitate that one estimate reward or what includes it in the aforementioned hadith; rather it is possible that this is established by another evidence. The answer to it: The meaning of his statement 'Verily reward is dependent on intention' is that reward is conditional upon intention by this hadith, because they use this hadith as evidence for it. Therefore, there is no doubt about the validity of his inference by saying 'So it is necessary to estimate...' etc. Third: That estimation is not necessary, because it is permissible for 'deeds' to be a metaphor for their ruling, so there is no need to delete anything.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage addresses three scholarly investigations (abhath) that challenge the Hanafi position on niyyah and reward. First: Some Hanafis (later ones) held that reward can be obtained even without intention, contradicting the claim of consensus. The commentator dismisses this as weak and asserts the consensus of the early scholars. Second: An objection that the hadith might not be the only proof; the commentator insists that the hadith is the primary evidence, thus requiring its interpretation. Third: The possibility that the word 'deeds' is metaphorical for 'ruling of deeds' so no deletion is needed; but the commentator does not accept this as it would still imply the hadith is about ruling, which he has already divided into worldly and otherworldly. The overall point is that the Hanafi interpretation is sound against these objections.

Key Terms
محيط (al-Muhit): A Hanafi fiqh reference: al-Muhit al-Burhani.
خزانة المفتين (Khizanat al-Muftin): A collection of Hanafi fatwas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that intention is required for reward, and objections based on later minority opinions are not accepted.

The Hadith of Intentions: Reward vs. Validity in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on the hadith, explaining its scope as reward, not validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi interpretation of the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' regarding reward and validity, and explain why the hadith is interpreted primarily for reward.

Core Idea
The hadith primarily concerns reward in the Hereafter; validity is implied but not directly proven by it.

The famous hadith 'Actions are by intentions' is often cited to prove that intention is necessary for the validity of acts. However, Hanafi scholars have nuanced its interpretation. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that the hadith, by consensus, is about reward (thawab), not directly about validity (sihha). Understanding this distinction is crucial to applying the hadith correctly in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus
Recognize that scholars have agreed (ijma') that reward depends on intention. This sets the foundation for the interpretation.
The consensus is based on this very hadith.
2
Understand the problem
The apparent meaning of the hadith would negate the existence of any action without intention, which is impractical and contradicts known rulings (e.g., wudu without niyya is still valid physically).
Thus, the hadith cannot be taken literally for all aspects.
3
Apply the principle of necessity
The interpretation must be restricted to what is necessary: reward. Since the consensus already establishes reward requires intention, the hadith is interpreted accordingly, avoiding absurdity.
This is based on the legal maxim: 'What is established by necessity is measured by the extent of necessity.'
4
Analyze the objections
The text presents five objections (abhas) from hypothetical critics, each followed by a Hanafi response. Work through each to see how the Hanafi position is defended.
Key points: estimation (taqdir) is preferred over metaphor; the hadith itself is the proof for reward; context of emigration supports reward interpretation.
5
Evaluate the dispute
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement: some later scholars claimed reward is possible without intention, but the majority (muttaqaddimun) reject this. The relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that reward requires intention.
This dispute does not affect validity; it only concerns reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأخروي مراد في هذا الحديث بالإجماع، فإنّهم أجمعوا على أنه لا ثواب إلا بالنية. فإذا قيل : حكم الأعمال بالنيات وأريد به الثواب صح هذا الكـلام مـن غـيـر ضرورة إلى أن يحمل على العموم، ويجعل شاملا للصحة، فلا يحمل الحكم على المعنى الأعم ؛ لأنَّ ما ثبت بالضرورة يتقدّر بقدر الضرورة، والاحتياج إلى حذف الثواب أو ما يعمه إنّما وقع لعدم استقامة ظاهر الحديث المقتضي لنفي وجود الأعمال بدون النية، فلما اندفع ذلك بإرادة الثواب، لا يراد غيره.
فاحفظ هذا كله، ولا تنظر إلى تقريرات الناظرين كأخي جلبي، والسيد مهدي، وعبد الله الهروي وغيرهم، فإنهم قرروا كلام الشارح هاهنا بما لا ينبغي كما فصلنا ذلك في «السعاية» (١).
وفي المقام أبحاث :
الأول : إن دعوى كون الثواب منوطاً بالنيَّةِ اتَّفاقاً ممنوعة، فقد ذكر في «المحيط» و«خزانة المفتين»: «إنّهم تكلموا في أنه إذا ترك النية هل يجد ثواب الوضوء أم لا ؟ فقال أكثر المتقدمين: لا يثاب وقال بعض المتأخرين : يثاب). والجواب عنه : إنّ القول بحصول الثواب بدون النية لما كان ضعيفاً لم يعتبر به ، وادعى بالاتفاق.
والثاني : إنَّ كون الثواب منوطاً بالنيَّة لا يوجب أن يقدر الثواب، أو ما يشمله في الحديث المذكور الجواز أن يكون ذلك ثابتاً بدليل آخر. والجواب عنه : إن معنى قوله : «إنّ الثواب منوطاً بالنيَّة» أن الثواب موقوف على النية بهذا الحديث ؛ لأنهم يستدلون عليه بهذا الحديث، وحينئذ لا ريب في صحة تفريعه بقوله : «فلا بد أن يقدر ...» الخ.
والثالث : إن التقدير ليس بلازم لجواز أن تكون الأعمال مجازاً عن حكمها، فلا يحتاج إلى حذف شيء.
فإن قيل : حكم الأعمال بالنيَّات، ويراد به الثواب ، صُدِّقَ الكلام، فلا دلالة له على الصحة.
والجواب عنه : إن التقدير هاهنا أرجحُ من ارتكاب المجاز، والراجح من حيث هو راجح لا يعدل عنه إلا عن ضرورة. والرابع : إنه لما ثبت توقف الثواب على النيَّة بالإجماع، وهو دليل من الأدلة الشرعية، أي حاجةٍ إلى أن يحمل الحديث المذكور على ما حمله لإثباته، بل الواجب حينئذ أن يقدّر شيء لم يثبت حاله من الإجماع وهوا الصحة ؛ ليفيد الحديث فائدة جديدة
والجواب عنه : إنّ الإجماع إنما هو على توقف الثواب على النية بهذا الحديث ، فلا بد أن يقدَّرَ الثواب أو ما يشمله ليوافق شاهد الإجماع الإجماع. والخامس : إن حذف الصحة أولى؛ لأن نفي الصحة أقرب إلى نفي وجودِ الأعمال بدون النية.
والجواب عنه : إنا لا نسلّم ذلك، فإنَّ سياق الحديث المذكور وهو قوله : «فمن كانت هجرته إلى الله ورسوله فهجرته إلى الله ورسوله ، ومَن كانت هجرته إلى دنيا يصيبها ، أو امرأة يتزوجها فهجرته إلى ما هاجر إليه» يرجح إرادة نفي الثواب
[١]قوله : فإن قيل ؛ هذا نقض على التقرير المذكور بإجرائه في غير الوسائل كالصلاة والصوم والزكاة ونحوها ، وحاصله : أن المجيب ذكر أنه لا دلالة للحديث المذكور على اشتراط النيَّة لصحة الوضوء، وبيّنه بأنّ المقدَّرَ فيه إن كان الثواب فظاهر، وإن كان أعم منه ، فإذا أريد الثواب اندفعت الحاجة، وصدق الكلام.
ومثل هذا الكلام يجري في سائر العبادات، فلا يدل الحديث على اشتراط النية لصحة العبادات المقصودة أيضاً، مع أنه ليس كذلك، فإنّ دليل اشتراط النية لصحتها عند المجيب أيضاً هو هذا الحديث، وبهذا التقرير يرتفع توهم أنه لا ضير في جريان التقرير المذكور في العبادات المقصودة، وعدم ثبوتِ اشتراط النية بهذا الحديث لصحتها بثبوته بدلائل أخر كالإجماع ، وكقوله : ( وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ (١) وغير ذلك.
(۱) البينة : من الآية .
And the afterlife [reward] is intended in this hadith by consensus, for they have agreed that there is no reward except by intention. So if it is said, 'The judgment of actions is by intentions' and by it reward is meant, this statement is correct without necessity to interpret it generally or make it inclusive of validity. Thus, the ruling is not applied to the broader meaning, because that which is established by necessity is measured by the extent of necessity, and the need to delete 'reward' or what encompasses it only occurred due to the lack of consistency in the apparent meaning of the hadith necessitating the negation of the existence of actions without intention. Since that is repelled by intending reward, nothing else is intended.
So preserve all this, and do not look at the explanations of the critics like Akhi Chalabi, Sayyid Mahdi, Abdullah al-Harawi and others, for they have explained the words of the commentator here improperly, as we have detailed in 'al-Si'aya' (1).
And here there are investigations:
First: The claim that reward is contingent upon intention by agreement is not granted, for it is mentioned in 'al-Muhit' and 'Khizanat al-Muftin': 'They discussed that if one omits the intention, does he receive the reward of wudu or not? The majority of the early scholars said: He is not rewarded, and some later scholars said: He is rewarded.' The answer to this: The statement that reward is obtained without intention, since it is weak, is not considered, and is claimed by agreement.
Second: That reward is contingent upon intention does not necessitate that reward or what encompasses it be estimated in the aforementioned hadith; it is possible that this is established by another proof. The answer: The meaning of his statement, 'Reward is contingent upon intention,' is that reward is dependent on intention by this hadith, because they use this hadith as evidence for it. Then there is no doubt about the validity of his derivation in his statement, 'It is necessary to estimate...' etc.
Third: That estimation is not necessary, because it is permissible that actions are a metaphor for their ruling, so there is no need to delete anything.
If it is said: 'The judgment of actions is by intentions, and reward is meant,' the statement is confirmed, so it has no indication of validity.
The answer: Estimation here is more preponderant than resorting to metaphor, and the preponderant, as it is preponderant, is not deviated from except by necessity.
Fourth: Since the dependence of reward on intention is established by consensus, and it is a legal proof, what need is there to interpret the aforementioned hadith as he interpreted it to prove it? Rather, the duty then is to estimate something not established by consensus, namely validity, so that the hadith yields a new benefit.
The answer: Consensus is only on the dependence of reward on intention by this hadith, so it is necessary to estimate reward or what encompasses it, so that the testimony of consensus matches consensus.
Fifth: Deleting validity is more appropriate, because negating validity is closer to negating the existence of actions without intention.
The answer: We do not concede that, for the context of the aforementioned hadith, namely his statement: 'So whoever emigrated to God and His Messenger, his emigration is to God and His Messenger; and whoever emigrated for worldly gain or a woman to marry, his emigration is to that for which he emigrated,' gives preponderance to intending the negation of reward.
[1] His statement: If it is said: This is a refutation of the aforementioned explanation by applying it to other than means like prayer, fasting, zakat, and the like. Its summary: The responder mentioned that the aforementioned hadith does not indicate the condition of intention for the validity of wudu, and he explained that if what is estimated is reward, it is apparent; and if it is more general, then when reward is intended the need is repelled and the statement is true.
And such speech applies to all other acts of worship, so the hadith does not indicate the condition of intention for the validity of intended acts of worship either, even though it is not so, for the proof of conditioning intention for their validity according to the responder is also this hadith. And with this explanation, the misconception is removed that there is no harm in the aforementioned explanation applying to intended acts of worship, and that the condition of intention is not established by this hadith for their validity, since it is established by other proofs like consensus and His statement: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, being sincere to Him in religion' (Al-Bayyinah: 5) and others.
(1) Al-Bayyinah: from the verse.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the famous hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' (Actions are by intentions). The author, a Hanafi scholar, argues that the hadith primarily concerns reward (thawab) in the Hereafter, not the validity (sihha) of acts themselves, though it indirectly implies that intention is necessary for validity because valid acts are prerequisite for reward. He begins by stating that there is consensus that reward requires intention. He then rejects interpretations that claim the hadith also explicitly covers validity, explaining that the apparent meaning of the hadith would negate the existence of any action without intention, which is impractical; thus, it must be restricted to reward. The author warns against incorrect explanations by later scholars. He then presents five objections (abhas) and their answers. The first objection notes that the consensus on reward is disputed: some later Hanafi scholars hold that reward can be obtained without intention. The author responds that this view is weak and disregarded. The second objection suggests that the dependence of reward on intention might be proven by another proof, not this hadith. The author insists that the hadith is the primary evidence. The third objector argues that estimation (taqdir) is unnecessary; actions could be metaphorical for their rulings. The author prefers estimation over metaphorical interpretation. The fourth objection states that since consensus already established the dependence of reward on intention, interpreting the hadith to prove it adds nothing new; rather, the hadith should be used to prove validity. The author answers that the consensus itself is based on this hadith, so the hadith must be interpreted to confirm it. The fifth objector argues that deleting validity is better because negating validity is closer to negating existence. The author counters by citing the context of the hadith about emigration, which clearly speaks about worldly versus spiritual reward, thus supporting the interpretation about reward. Finally, a marginal note addresses a potential refutation: if the hadith only indicates reward, then how can it be used to prove the necessity of intention for all acts of worship? The author explains that the same principle applies: the hadith is the basis for requiring intention in all acts, and this is supported by other proofs like consensus and the Quranic verse 'وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا الله مخلصين له الدين' (They were commanded to worship Allah with sincere devotion).

Key Terms
النية (al-niyya): The intention in the heart that distinguishes acts of worship from habits and differentiates between the purposes of acts.
الثواب (al-thawāb): The reward from Allah in the afterlife for a righteous deed performed with correct intention.
الصحة (al-ṣiḥḥa): Legal validity of an act, meaning it fulfills its requirements and exempts from further obligation.
الإجماع (al-ijmāʿ): Consensus of scholars on a ruling, in this case that reward depends on intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the majority of early Hanafi scholars say about performing wudu without niyya?
A) They receive no reward.
B) They receive reward.
C) The wudu is valid but no reward.
D) The wudu is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' is interpreted by Hanafi scholars to primarily establish that reward depends on intention; validity is derived from other proofs.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
